My favorite example of this kind of distribution and diffusion taking longer than you would think for a very fundamental technology—well, now this is my favorite example, but my second favorite example is oil. I interviewed Daniel Yergin, who wrote this big book about the history of oil. In the 1860s, Drake strikes oil in Pennsylvania. It's not until the 1910s that the car is invented and the internal combustion engine is put into a thing which you sell millions of copies of. Until then, oil is just used for kerosene, which is just for lighting. The actual gas is just thrown away. In fact, when the lightbulb was invented, people were wondering whether Standard Oil was going to go bankrupt because the main use case had gone away. Neat. I mean, I always think of Julius Caesar's description of Britain. When the Romans first get to Britain, he says the people of Britain are so poor they can't afford to burn wood, so they burn rocks. And we know he's talking about coal. Oh, I thought it was satire. No, he's talking about coal. Hilarious. And they had coal in the days of Julius Caesar, but they didn't figure out its massive industrial utility until many, many, many years later. Yeah. There is this interesting question of why the Romans didn't have the Industrial Revolution, because they had these huge silver mines in Spain and elsewhere, but no coal. But I mean, you have the Industrial Revolution when you feel you need to. That's the thing about Gutenberg as well that a lot of people don't think about. People think Gutenberg was an inventor who invented a thing and then it had an impact. No. He was living in the middle of a library building boom in which there was a huge demand for books that spiked. He invented the invention in response to that cultural change. It isn't by chance that we got the printing press in 1450. There was a huge boom of library building starting in the 1410s, and inventors were trying to figure out ways to make books cheaper. They were making smaller books, they were using paper more, and paper surges before the Gutenberg movable-type printing press. So Gutenberg isn't a random genius out of nowhere; it was the moment that people needed more books. We were going to get the invention. One thing you say in passing in the book is that Martin Luther comes up at the exact right time. You've got Savonarola in the 1490s, another prophet type—the modern analog of someone like Khomeini in Iran who sets up a theocratic government—but he's too early. Then Machiavelli, you say, is too late because of the censorship already in place. What is the censorship that is in place by the time of Machiavelli? What is the alternative world where—well, Machiavelli, remember, is contemporary with Luther. It's just that he circulates his stuff very briefly and very privately. He doesn't want a pamphlet version of his ideas out there because he only wants Florence to have it. Luther hits the sweet spot when the pamphlet distribution network had just developed. Hence, when Savonarola printed pamphlets, they only circulated around Florence and its neighbors: Siena, Pisa. It took months for them to get farther, and his movement was quickly crushed. When Luther makes the 95 Theses public, they're in print in London 17 days after he releases them in Wittenberg. The pamphlet runners go vroom vroom vroom and get the news there, and things are printed overnight and come out that fast. But it seems like you're hinting that within the next two decades, there's a new censorship regime across Europe. Yeah, a new censorship regime responds. The censorship regime is very effective at shaping what is printed in books, but can never keep up with pamphlets. In the same way the government can pressure CNN, the government can't pressure random people on a social media network. You're not going to be able to keep up with that speed. One of the funny problems that the Inquisition always had when trying to persecute printers is that printers worked in the information distribution industry. They were the people who paid the news writers, whose job it was to move as fast as humanly possible between cities. Which meant that news always reached them first. If a printer was ever convicted by the Inquisition, they would find out before the Inquisition could possibly get there to arrest them. The Inquisition never succeeded at arresting printers; they always skipped town by the time the Inquisition got there. If you employ the news writers, you find out first what's going on. The Inquisition can't keep up. When we look at censorship, there's an intersection of four factors as to whether censorship is possible. One of them is law—is it legal for this censorship to happen? But another one is the technology—is it actually possible to censor this thing? You cannot censor whatever moves the information fastest, because it will move the information faster than you can move. Even if that one printer had to skip town, he will set up shop somewhere else. A new person will take over his shop, and the information will still move. So pamphlets become unpoliceable. You can try to police them, you can partially police them, but keeping pamphlets from moving around is impossible. They're anonymous, they're quick, they're produced overnight, and they move quickly. You just can't keep up with them. I guess couldn't they just punish print shops for publishing things? They could take a guess at what authorities won't like, and if they don't like it, they'll punish the shop—which is kind of how censorship in China works, for example. Yeah, they did. So you skip town. But the printer? Yeah, the printer skips town. And there is a cost to that; there's not "no human cost" to evading that. You've had to leave your home and friends behind and move to a new place, but they don't get you. It's very easy to deny that the pamphlet came from you at all. So the print industry proves very difficult to censor, and we're experiencing the same thing with social media right now. Everyone says, "Censor the pornography" or "Censor the hate speech," and they say, "We just can't; it's too fast, there's too much." There were too many pamphlets, though they could crack down on one particular shop. We have records of this—there's a brilliant analysis in Antoni Matis's book, *The Spectre of Skepticism in the Age of Enlightenment*. He has a great description from the notes of a raid on a clandestine bookshop. This wasn't the printer; this was the underground bookshop that was selling illegal books. They're raided, and it has all the details of how angry the people were about different things that the shop had. So there was censorship and there were crackdowns, but it was a censorship that could not actually prevent circulation. It could restrict it, it could make it harder, it could make it scary, but it couldn't prevent it. I guess before books become cheap, you've got people who, unless they are fantastically wealthy, are reading the same couple of books again and again through their lives. Cosimo de Medici's father owned, I think it was, 12 books. I want to understand the intellectual significance of rereading the exact same book again and again. Maybe the reason Petrarch loved Cicero so much is—imagine reading the same book like 20 times and hitting the same joke and meditating on it. There's got to be a difference in intellectual culture as a result of taking these things as the equivalent of the Bible. Yeah, well, and you really get to know the person intimately. You develop a personal relationship with the ancient author. You are participating in a conversation across the diaspora of time. It's a one-way conversation; you're responding to them, and the future will respond to you. But there is a great deal of intimacy. Petrarch talks about his friend Cicero and being betrayed by him. He finds new works of Cicero that he hadn't read, in which Cicero is not following his own stoic philosophical precepts. He is being petty and yelling at people about real estate and getting all upset after his daughter's death. You know how people get kind of manic when there's been a death in the family and start quarreling about everything? Cicero gets like that, and Petrarch is heartbroken. To him, it means even the wisest man in history could not conquer that urge to become irrational and petty in the face of grief. If even Cicero became irrational and petty, does that mean humanity is doomed to forever be that way? He talks about Cicero breaking his heart and his foot—because the book fell on his foot and broke it. He got a bad infection and was bedridden for months. Totally different topic, but around this time—I know we're jumping around—in 1492, Columbus comes to the New World. What is the reception of this news? Although I was just at a conference a week ago in which we confirmed that there's a Vatican document from like 1100 or maybe 1200 that recognizes the existence of Vinland, i.e., Canada. They got the information from the Vikings, but they thought it was just a little thing. But rediscovering the New World—is it today the equivalent of "we just found aliens"? Why wasn't it considered a more significant thing? When I teach my class on the 1490s, the students always have trouble wrapping their heads around the people thinking the New World isn't a big deal. A big part of it is that they find the Caribbean Islands and they find the coast, and they think this is small. The news comes back: "We've found something across the water to the west; it might be even as big as the Canary Islands." They don't realize they've found something the scale of Europe and Africa. That's part of it. Another part is that no matter how big and important something far away is, it's hard to bring your mind out of the petty squabbles happening right around you, especially when they feel like life or death. If it's 1492, France is about to invade Italy. Europe is about to be embroiled in the largest war it's seen in 50 years. The papacy has just been taken over by Spain, and Spain is suddenly trying to throw its weight around. The Ottomans have just invaded Italy and Hungary and might be coming again. Also, over there there's a new thing. "Okay, great, we'll worry about that when we're not having three wars at the same time." Then Martin Luther hits Europe like a ton of bricks when they still haven't even figured out that this is a continent and not an island. If you're in a country having a tumult, you worry a lot about its tumult, even if a larger one is happening in a faraway country. They're also inventing lots of new things, and it falls into that sphere. They're discovering the existence of sub-Saharan Africa, which they thought was just Ethiopia and nothing else. They're also discovering that the heart is a pump. The discovery of the New World, especially when they realize how big it is, becomes an intellectual challenge where they say, "Wait, does this mean all the maps we've had are wrong? Does this mean the ancients were wrong about geography?" Does it matter whether the earth goes around the sun when the French are invading right now and we need to get the defenses going and there's a giant civil war happening? It matters, but it also doesn't matter. In the same way that any decade is concerned by its tumults and fails to recognize the importance of what's around it, that's true of every decade. One fun game when I study the history of censorship—my next non-fiction book is going to be on the history of censorship. Whatever they're looking at, they're always wrong from our perspective about what they should be worried about. Here we are in the French Enlightenment—Voltaire and Rousseau and the Marquis de Sade are flying around Europe—and what is the Inquisition worried about? It's worried about Jansenist treatises about the nature of the Trinity. Jansenism is sort of like a Calvinist version of Catholicism. Do you want to have an incredibly terrifying authoritarian God who hates you but also have to obey the arbitrary Pope in Rome? Then Jansenism is for you. This was a heresy abroad in the Enlightenment, and they are so much more worried about Jansenism than they are about Voltaire. In Matis's book, he describes a raid on a clandestine shop. The authorities were like, "Voltaire? Fine. The banned Encyclopédie, which is going to revolutionize all thought in Europe? Fine. Rousseau? Fine. Oh, Jansenist treatises about the nature of the Trinity! Throw the book at these guys; this is the worst thing!" They are obsessed with this minor heresy. When the Encyclopédie is banned by Rome, Paris is commanded to ban it. But Paris loves this book. The king likes it, the queen likes it. She said it was so cool being able to look up the technology used to make her silk pantyhose. France allows it to circulate despite its content, but Rome says no. So they agree to a ceremonial burning. They march the Encyclopédie up to the fire, but then they get some Jansenist treatises and burn those instead. They want to burn this other thing. This is always true. If we had a time machine for the Inquisition in the 1540s, we would say, "Guys, Machiavelli—he's really important, he's revolutionary, you've got to be looking at this." Or we would say, "Lucretius's *De Rerum Natura*." There's a much more complex story told in my book which refers to Greenblatt. We would say, "Guys, you should censor this." We literally have letters of inquisitors writing to each other saying, "We don't need to bother censoring Lucretius; only learned people can read it." They thought learned people knew the false stuff was false, so it could circulate. What they worried about were the fine-tune minutiae of Protestantism. The 1545 edition of the Index of Banned Books says it will put the names of arch-heretics in all caps. When I first read that, I wanted to see my favorite heretics in all caps. I flipped to M, and Machiavelli is not in all caps. He was not important enough from their position. The all-caps authors are all minor Protestant theologians. They're people like Calvin and Zwingli and Luther—people who are doing stuff that we would say does not matter today. An age is always wrong about what ideas and what changes are the really big ones. People are always much more worried about things like, "Which princess is the Prince of Spain going to marry? This will determine whether Spain is annexed by Germany! This is the most important thing that has ever happened!" People say, "We've discovered another continent," and they say, "We don't care; we just want to know who's going to marry Charles." That is a very profound observation. It was interesting to learn from your book that, of all the thousands of people killed during the Inquisition, one guy was executed for science-related stuff—well, for the most extensive experimental laboratory, I should say. Danielle Macuglia is the scholar there. He works on the Inquisition in the immediate aftermath of Galileo. They saw themselves as guarantors of truth and of accuracy in information. So they decided after Galileo that they had a duty to verify the truth of the books they were sent to censor. If people were going to be doing mechanical experiments, they decided they needed to repeat the mechanical experiments to see whether they were true. So, effectively, the Inquisition invented peer review. They invented a second laboratory trying to recreate the results of the first. There are these amazing people who by day are inquisitors and by night are going home to write their own scientific treatises as they do these experiments. It's not what we expect, but history is never what we expect. Seems like a good place to close. Ada, thank you very much. Thank you.

Former President Barack Obama gives remarks at memorial for Rev. Jesse Jackson.

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·29:01 ·524 views · 2026-03-06
Barack Obama (Former President) Jesse Jackson (Civil Rights Leader)
Former President Barack Obama honored Reverend Jesse Jackson as a transformational "messenger of hope" whose courage and political audacity dismantled racial barriers and established the foundational path for the first Black presidency.
TL;DR Former President Barack Obama honored Reverend Jesse Jackson as a transformational "messenger of hope" whose courage and political audacity dismantled racial barriers and established the foundational path for the first Black presidency. Biblical Inspiration: Obama framed...

TL;DR

Former President Barack Obama honored Reverend Jesse Jackson as a transformational "messenger of hope" whose courage and political audacity dismantled racial barriers and established the foundational path for the first Black presidency.

The "Send Me" Legacy: Early Activism and Civil Rights

  • Biblical Inspiration: Obama framed Jackson’s life through the Book of Isaiah, noting that whenever the dispossessed needed a champion, Jackson answered the call with "Send me."
  • Defying Segregation: Born into the "oppressive cloud" of the Jim Crow South, Jackson refused to accept the limited "lot in life" assigned to him.
      • Early Defiance: As a young man, he led seven Black students into a whites-only library; the subsequent arrests eventually led to the facility's integration.
  • Economic Justice: Through Operation Breadbasket and Operation PUSH, Jackson recognized that civil rights were "empty promises" without economic equity.
      • Corporate Accountability: He led boycotts to challenge hiring and contracting policies, advocating for unions and fair wage campaigns.
      • Intersectional Solidarity: He argued that if the "have-nots and have-little-bits" found common cause across racial lines, everyone would benefit.

The Rainbow Coalition and Political Transformation

  • Expanding the Electorate: Jackson’s 1984 and 1988 presidential campaigns focused on the "Rainbow Coalition," a diverse assembly of Black, Latino, Asian, and First American voters, alongside family farmers, environmentalists, and gay rights activists.
  • Institutional Change: Jackson’s campaigns forced the Democratic Party to end "winner-take-all" delegate distribution.
      • Impact on Future Candidates: This rule change allowed "underdogs and outsiders" like Bill Clinton and Bernie Sanders to remain competitive and build momentum.
  • Validating Outsiders: Obama recounted watching Jackson in a 1984 debate on a small black-and-white TV, realizing that Jackson "owned that stage" and sent a message to every outsider that they belonged in the highest rooms of power.

Mentorship and the Bridge to the Presidency

  • Paving the Way for Obama: Obama explicitly credited Jackson’s "courage and audacity" for creating the political environment where a young Black senator from Chicago’s South Side could be taken seriously as a presidential candidate two decades later.
  • Chicago Roots: Jackson’s activism and the campaign of Harold Washington were the primary catalysts that drew Obama to Chicago to work as a community organizer in neighborhoods like Roseland and Woodlawn.
  • A Master Class in Power: Obama described Saturday mornings at PUSH headquarters as "master classes" where Jackson would:
      • Make complicated economic and historical concepts plain for bus drivers and teachers alike.
      • Offer "hands-on" mentorship to young leaders, giving them the confidence to contribute to the movement.

A Call to Action Against Modern Cynicism

  • Confronting Current Challenges: Obama addressed the present political climate, citing "assaults on democratic institutions," the denigration of expertise, and the celebration of "greed and bigotry."
  • Rejecting Cynicism: He urged the audience not to succumb to the temptation of "putting your head down" but to follow Jackson’s example of taking the "harder path."
  • Individual Responsibility: The briefing concluded with a call for citizens to serve as "heralds of change" in their own schools, workplaces, and neighborhoods.

***

Notable Quotes

"It was because of that path that he had laid, because of his courage, his audacity, that two decades later, a young black senator from Chicago’s South Side would even be taken seriously as a candidate for the presidential nomination."

— Barack Obama

"He instinctively understood that individual success meant nothing unless everybody was free."

— Barack Obama

"In his ideas, in his platform, in his analysis, in his intelligence... Jesse hadn’t just held his own, he had owned that stage. He wasn’t an intruder, he wasn’t a pretender, he belonged on that stage."

— Barack Obama

"If the have-nots and the have-little-bits ever learned to make common cause across racial lines instead of fighting each other over breadcrumbs, everybody would benefit."

— Barack Obama

My seatmate from the Senate, who came on to be the 44th president of the United States, Chicago, ladies and gentlemen, President Barack Obama. It's good to be home. I love you back. The book... the book of Isaiah... where you at? No, see, I believe in the Constitution. The book of Isaiah. God is looking for a messenger to guide a hardened and resistant people. And the Lord asks: "Whom shall I send? And who will go with us?" To which Isaiah replies: "Here I am, Lord. Send me." To Mother Jackson and the Jackson family, to President and Mrs. Biden, President and Secretary Clinton, Vice President Harris, Pastor Gates, my old friend—though he looking good—Reverend Meeks, Reverend Jenkins. It is an honor to join you today to celebrate the Reverend Jesse Louis Jackson. A man who, when the poor and the dispossessed needed a champion and a country needed healing, stepped forward again and again and again and said: "Send me." Reverend Jackson's immense gifts were apparent at an early age, even if his circumstances conspired to try to hold him back. Born out of wedlock to a teenage mom, he grew up under the oppressive cloud of segregation, confined to schools, sports facilities, and movie theaters that were separate and unequal. It was a world where on Thanksgiving, he'd have to wait for his mother to come home on the bus carrying leftovers from the dinner she had to cook for somebody else. It was a world designed to tell a child that he or she could only go so far; that to think otherwise would be foolish or dangerous, and that wisdom required you to accept your lot in life. And young Jesse refused to accept that verdict. He was a born leader, an athlete, a talker—knew how to talk—star quarterback, student body president. He could have succeeded within the confines that were determined for him and had a successful life. But like so many of his generation, so many extraordinary civil rights leaders in the late 50s and 60s, that Joshua generation, he instinctively understood that individual success meant nothing unless everybody was free. So he became inspired by the bus boycotts in Montgomery. He led seven black students into the whites-only library, sitting down and getting arrested for reading. Think about that. The library closed, but then it reopened. And when it did, it was open to everyone. "Send me," Jesse said, even as a young man, and the world got a little bit better. By the time Reverend Jackson graduated from college, he attended Chicago Theological Seminary. He knew the nature of his calling, and he became, as everybody knows, the youngest member of SCLC, assigned to lead Operation Breadbasket here in Chicago, right around the time that Isaiah Thomas is talking about. It was during this period, and especially after Dr. King's death, when the optimism of the early movement had begun to fade and leadership had begun to fracture. When the country seemed to have grown bored, gotten weary of the idea of justice and equality and moved on to other concerns, Reverend Jackson rose above despair and kept that righteous flame alive. Through Operation PUSH, he led boycotts and challenged corporate policies around hiring and contracting, recognizing that civil rights without economic justice was an empty promise. He backed unions in their organizing efforts and activists in fair wage campaigns, understanding that if the have-nots and the have-little-bits ever learned to make common cause across racial lines instead of fighting each other over breadcrumbs, everybody would benefit. He helped to register millions of voters. He fought against biases in the criminal justice system. He drew attention to local abuses of power and called folks at the national level to account. And by the early 80s, he was delivering that message of change and hope across the globe, freeing hostages from captivity and fighting to end apartheid in South Africa. Then in 1984, as the powers that be in Washington were rolling back hard-won progress, slashing the social safety net, when more and more folks were getting left behind and greed was being trumpeted as a virtue—see, we been there before—he stepped forward once again and said: "Send me." He ran for the presidency of the United States of America. I had just graduated from college during that first campaign, and I was living in New York at the time. I was working to pay off my student loans, eating a lot of tuna fish and Campbell soup. If I went to a diner, you know, I'd grab some extra crackers and put them in my pocket. I was inspired by the civil rights movement, and I had my mind to work for social justice. But even though I was full of good intentions, I was uncertain of how to serve and fighting off self-doubt. I remember how at the time, plenty of people, including, I'm sorry, plenty of black folks, were dismissing Jesse's chances, suggesting, oh, he just wants attention, he can only get black votes. But then I remember one night sitting in my janky apartment, and I got an old black and white TV with the rabbit ears, and I'm kind of jiggering around, and it's about this big—I know young people can't imagine but the TV was about this big—and I'm watching the Democratic primary debate between him and Walter Mondale and Gary Hart. And I remember how when that debate was over, I turned off that TV and I thought the same thing that I know a lot of people thought that night, even if they didn't want to admit it: that in his ideas, in his platform, in his analysis, in his intelligence, in his insight, Jesse hadn't just held his own, he had owned that stage. He wasn't an intruder, he wasn't a pretender, he belonged on that stage. And the message he sent to a 22-year-old child of a single mother with a funny name, an outsider, was that maybe there wasn't any place, any room where we didn't belong. And that message of fairness and dignity, of justice and hope, that's what the Rainbow Coalition was all about. In 1984 and then again in 1988, Jesse didn't just speak to black folks, he spoke to white folks and Latinos and Asian Americans and the First Americans. He spoke to family farmers and environmentalists. He spoke to gay rights activists when nobody was talking to gay rights activists, and blue-collar workers. He gave them the same message, that they mattered, that their voices and their votes counted. He invited them to believe, he invited us to believe in our own power to change America for the better. Now, by the delegate count, Jesse's two candidacies ultimately came up short. But he paved the road for so many others to follow. Doug Wilder became the first elected black governor. Carol Moseley Braun went to the US Senate. Because of Jesse, the Democratic Party changed its rules, ending the winner-take-all distribution of delegates during presidential primaries, which meant underdogs and outsiders like Bill Clinton or Bernie Sanders could stay competitive and build momentum instead of getting knocked out early. It was because of that path that he had laid, because of his courage, his audacity, that two decades later, a young black senator from Chicago's South Side would even be taken seriously as a candidate for the presidential nomination. I still credit that first run of Jesse's, and Harold Washington's campaign, for drawing me to Chicago. I didn't know anybody when I first arrived. I was working as a community organizer, as it so happens, in neighborhoods right around here: Roseland, Woodlawn, Altgeld, West Pullman. I met these young preachers like Meeks and some crazy priest named Pfleger. I guess Jenkins was running around, but he's kind of young, he came a little bit later. So I'm going to churches and I'm listening to all these amazing preachers. It was hard work, and half the time I didn't know what I was doing and progress was slow. I definitely didn't know how I was going to survive these Chicago winters, because I grew up in Hawaii. But I do remember heading to the PUSH headquarters some Saturday mornings to listen and learn from Reverend Jackson. And y'all remember how on some mornings, if there was a major issue going on, the room would be packed to the rafters. Sometimes some celebrity just wanders in. Then some mornings it was kind of low-key. Either way, though, announcements would be made and Reverend Barrow would say a prayer. And Jesse would sit there and then finally he'd gather himself up and stand, and he'd start kind of slow, you know. Sometimes he'd talk kind of low so you couldn't quite catch what he was saying, so everybody lean in. Over time, it was that same boundless energy that would emerge, and that same passion, and that same insight. He'd offer you a master class in economics on a Saturday morning. He'd give you a seminar in American history and American politics. He'd make complicated things plain. He'd tell stories that would make you laugh one minute and cry the next. Whether you were a bus driver or a teacher or a business leader or a young organizer, you came away from those meetings with a better understanding of how the world worked, how power worked. And more importantly, he made you believe that if we came together, we could make the world work better. Now, we've been talking about Jesse telling us "We are somebody," "I am somebody." And he wasn't just talking to young black boys like Isaiah, though he loved them fiercely. He wasn't just talking to young black girls to help them believe, though he believed in them. He was talking about everyone who was left out. Everyone who was forgotten. Everyone who was unseen. Everyone who was unheard. And in that sense, he was expressing the very essence of what our democracy should be: the ideals at the very heart of the American experiment. The belief that regardless of what we look like or how we worship, regardless of where our ancestors come from or how much money we got, we're all part of the American family. We're all endowed with the same inalienable rights to life and liberty and the pursuit of happiness. We're all obligated to answer the call and step forward and take responsibility for making wrongs right, for caring for our neighbors, and for bringing the reality of America a step closer to its glorious ideals. Answering that call isn't always easy. Reverend Jackson and his family knew that better than most. To do what he did, he would endure all kinds of hatred and setbacks and betrayals and doubters and death threats. He would sacrifice, as Jim Reynolds pointed out, the leisure and comfort of what was available to him as a far more financially secure life. Those sacrifices were not his alone. They were shared by his wife and his children, and they bore that burden with grace and strength. I got to know the Reverend and his family over the years and as I watched his children follow his example of service, I came to appreciate the conviction that drove him and that he had passed on, the faith that guided him and that he had passed on. It was a faith that didn't waver, a flame that burned bright even as his body began to fail. The last time he and I had a chance to visit in person, he was already ailing. It was getting difficult for him to stand, difficult for him to speak. We were in Hyde Park in a hotel room. I embraced him and figured we'd just have a low-key visit, maybe he'd need some rest. And he starts coming up with this project and this initiative and issues I needed to look into, and here's some commentary that he would suggest and some phrasing that he thought might work and maybe we might co-write an article. Listening to him, I couldn't help smiling because it took me back. I started reminiscing with him about those Saturday mornings at PUSH and the breakfast that he'd host, you know, away from the full meeting. I didn't get a chance to go to that until I was a state senator and Brad Meeks invited me. So I was tagging along with Meeks and... And he'd pull out a piece of paper, you know, he'd scribbled some stuff and he'd start passing out assignments and pulling together working groups and asking for updates on the latest campaign. I'll admit, let's face it, those sessions could run a little long and did not always follow Robert's Rules of Order. But when Jesse called your name and acknowledged you in that room, especially if you were young, especially if you were just coming up, especially if you weren't sure you could pull off what you thought you might be able to do, especially when you needed help, when he did that, you stood up a little straighter. You knew you'd been seen by this giant. He'd laid hands on you and let you know you had a contribution to make. So that day, I told him how much that meant to me and how much it had meant to so many lives that he impacted. I told him thank you, because I'd always be grateful for that legacy of hope. I know I've gone long. It was going to be much shorter until Isaiah spoke. I figure if a Detroit Piston can take up this much time... It was beautiful, and I mean that. Love me some Isaiah. We are living in a time when it can be hard to hope. Each day we wake up to some new assault on our democratic institutions. Another setback to the idea of the rule of law. An offense to common decency. Every day you wake up to things you just didn't think were possible. Each day we're told by those in high office to fear each other and to turn on each other, and that some Americans count more than others, and that some don't even count at all. Everywhere we see greed and bigotry being celebrated and bullying and mockery masquerading as strength. We see science and expertise denigrated while ignorance and dishonesty and cruelty and corruption are reaping untold rewards. Every single day we see that. And it's hard to hope in those moments. So it may be tempting to get discouraged, to give into cynicism. It may be tempting for some to compromise with power and grab what you can, or even for good people to maybe just put your head down and wait for the storm to pass. But this man, Reverend Jesse Louis Jackson, inspires us to take a harder path. His voice calls on each of us to be heralds of change, to be messengers of hope, to step forward and say: "Send me." Wherever we have a chance to make an impact, whether it's in our schools or our workplaces or our neighborhoods or our cities. Not for fame, not for glory, or because success is guaranteed, but because it gives our life purpose. Because it aligns with what our faith tells us God demands. And because if we don't step up, no one else will. How fortunate we were that Jesse Jackson answered that call. What a great debt we owe to him. May God bless Reverend Jackson. May he rest in eternal peace. God bless you.

TL;DR

The dismissal of Kristi Noem and the expansion of the Iran conflict highlight a period of administrative chaos and economic volatility, while the AI sector sees a massive shift in user trust from OpenAI toward Anthropic following controversial military partnerships.

The Dismissal of Kristi Noem and Republican Infighting

  • Kristi Noem's Exit: Homeland Security Secretary Kristi Noem has been removed from her post and reassigned to a "Special Envoy to the Shield of the Americas" role, which Scott Galloway likened to a "bad Marvel movie."
      • The Catalyst: Galloway reports the firing was triggered not by her personal scandals or controversial "dog-killing" stories, but by her spending upwards of $200 million on self-promoting ads, which Donald Trump viewed as an attempt to advance her own presidential ambitions.
      • Senate Rejection: Republicans in the Senate, including Thom Tillis (R-NC), signaled their lack of support for Noem during recent congressional hearings, effectively giving the White House permission to move on from her.
  • New DHS Leadership: Senator Markwayne Mullin (R-OK) has been named as her replacement. Senator Tillis noted the move would "restore competence" and joked that a major positive of Mullin is that "he likes dogs."

Iran Conflict and Economic Instability

  • Military Escalation: Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth warned that the war in Iran is "far from over" and "only just begun," despite pushback from European leaders and segments of the MAGA base.
  • Economic Fallout:
      • Energy Prices: US gas prices saw their largest single-day spike in three years this week; oil prices have risen roughly $11 per barrel (a 10-12% increase).
      • Market Fragility: While markets often rebound after wars, the current uncertainty poses an "existential threat" to US economic stability as the 2026 midterms approach.
  • Geopolitical Shift: Galloway argues the US is losing its status as the "operating system" of the world. By acting unilaterally and bypassing allies or Congress, the US has shifted from being "the cop" to being "the knock at the door" that other nations fear.

The AI Divide: OpenAI vs. Anthropic

  • OpenAI’s Trust Crisis: CEO Sam Altman admitted that the rollout of a Pentagon deal made the company look "opportunistic and sloppy" and acknowledged OpenAI has no control over how the Defense Department uses its software.
      • User Backtrack: ChatGPT uninstalls surged 295% the day after the Pentagon deal was announced.
  • The Anthropic Alternative: Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei has positioned his company as a principled alternative, refusing to engage in "dictator-style praise" of the administration.
      • Commercial Upside: Anthropic’s annual recurring revenue reportedly jumped from $14 billion to $19 billion in a single week as users migrated from OpenAI.
      • Prediction: Galloway predicts a "raft of CEOs" will follow Amodei’s lead over the next 30 days, finding the "testicles" to say no to the administration’s demands.

Texas Primaries and the Populist Shift

  • Primary Results: The Texas GOP Senate primary between John Cornyn and Ken Paxton has become the most expensive in history, with nearly $100 million spent on ads.
  • Democratic Turnout: 2.1 million Democrats turned out for the Texas primary, significantly outperforming expectations. James Talarico’s victory speech targeted the "billionaire class," signaling a shift toward populist rhetoric.
  • Inequality Data: The net worth of the top 0.1% of Americans doubled from 2020 to 2025, now accounting for 14.4% of total household wealth ($24.9 trillion). The top 10% of households are now responsible for 50% of all US spending.

Media Consolidation and "Burger Wars"

  • Paramount/Warner Merger: FCC Chair Brendan Carr indicated the merger would likely face a smooth regulatory path. However, Fitch Ratings has cut Paramount’s credit rating to "junk status" due to its massive debt (estimated between $75B and $100B).
  • Consolidation Pressures: Galloway and Swisher anticipate a merger between CNN and CBS is inevitable due to economic pressures, with Mark Thompson potentially leading the combined entity.
  • Health and GLP-1s: Amidst social media "burger wars" between McDonald's and Burger King, Galloway argues that GLP-1 drugs (like Ozempic) represent a more transformative economic force than AI, potentially disrupting the fast-food industry and reducing long-term healthcare costs.

Asia in 2050 Roundtable: Challenges Ahead for Central Banks in Asia

IMF(201.0K) ·63:50 ·705 views · 2026-03-06
Kristalina Georgieva (Managing Director, IMF) Ekniti Nitithanprapas (Director-General, Excise Department, Thailand)
To sustain growth through 2050, Asian economies must pivot from export-led manufacturing toward regional integration, service-oriented sectors, and digital innovation while institutionalizing macroeconomic stability to survive a "new normal" of constant global shocks.
TL;DR To sustain growth through 2050, Asian economies must pivot from export-led manufacturing toward regional integration, service-oriented sectors, and digital innovation while institutionalizing macroeconomic stability to survive a "new normal" of constant global shocks. ...

TL;DR

To sustain growth through 2050, Asian economies must pivot from export-led manufacturing toward regional integration, service-oriented sectors, and digital innovation while institutionalizing macroeconomic stability to survive a "new normal" of constant global shocks.

Macroeconomic Stability and Institutional Reform

  • The "New Normal" of Uncertainty:
      • Nandalal Weerasinghe (Governor, Central Bank of Sri Lanka) noted that the primary lesson of the 2022 Sri Lankan crisis was the danger of ignoring macro-financial stability. Building fiscal and external buffers is now a prerequisite for navigating global volatility.
      • Institutional Independence: Strengthening the independence of Central Banks and implementing Public Financial Management Acts are seen as critical "landmark reforms" to prevent fiscal dominance over monetary policy.
  • Transparency and Social Trust:
      • Policy Communication: Weerasinghe emphasized that painful reforms (e.g., a 700-basis point rate hike in a single day) only succeed if the government communicates "truthfully and with credibility" to the public to prevent social unrest.
      • Ethical Foundations: Kristalina Georgieva (IMF Managing Director) argued that economic success depends as much on an "ethical foundation" and community unity as it does on macroeconomic policy.
  • Regulatory "House Cleaning":
      • Ekniti Nitithanprapas (Finance Minister, Thailand) advocated for cutting "red tape" and obsolete laws. Thailand is pursuing OECD membership specifically to use external standards as a catalyst for internal structural reform.

Technological Advancement and Artificial Intelligence

  • AI Productivity Dividends:
      • Growth Potential: AI is projected to lift productivity by 0.2%–0.8% in emerging Asia and 0.5%–1.0% in advanced Asian economies.
      • The Power Requirement: Panelists warned that the energy consumption of AI is massive; if this energy is not clean, the technological revolution could conflict with climate goals.
  • Three-Layer Digital Foundation:
      • Ekniti Nitithanprapas proposed a unified regional framework:
          • Layer 1 (Foundations): Unified digital IDs, signatures, and payment systems.
          • Layer 2 (Platforms): Governance for data sharing, consent, and fraud protection.
          • Layer 3 (Shared Services): Interoperable services, such as cross-border digital health records.
  • Technology as a Social Leveler:
      • Skill Bridge Programs: Thailand and Singapore are investing in "Skill Bridge" initiatives to connect low-income workers and SMEs with AI tools to prevent a "K-shaped" recovery where only large corporations benefit.

Regional Integration and Trade Evolution

  • Underutilized Regional Potential:
      • Intra-regional Trade: Georgieva noted that ASEAN trade is only 22% internal, compared to 60% in the EU. Eliminating non-tariff barriers could provide a 4% boost to regional GDP.
      • From Competition to Coordination: Amir Hamzah Azizan (Minister of Finance, Malaysia) highlighted the Johor-Singapore Economic Zone as a model where neighboring countries co-attract FDI rather than competing for it, creating a larger "addressable market."
  • The Asian Power Grid:
      • Energy Integration: There is a strong push for an integrated power grid (e.g., linking hydroelectricity from Laos to Singapore) to reduce total system costs and facilitate the green transition.
  • Shifting Economic Engines:
      • Internal Consumption: Large economies, specifically China, are urged to pivot away from goods exports toward domestic consumption to sustain growth in a fragmented global trade environment.

Climate Resilience and Demographic Shifts

  • The "Lego Approach" to Disaster Preparedness:
      • Standardization: Georgieva proposed that Asia adopt a "Lego concept" for disaster response—standardizing equipment and training so countries can instantly connect resources during floods or cyclones.
  • Climate Mitigation and Carbon Markets:
      • Ekniti Nitithanprapas suggested that Asia develop a unified carbon market and mandatory carbon taxes/ETS similar to Europe, rather than individual voluntary systems.
  • Managing Demographic Divergence:
      • Aging Populations: In countries like Japan and Thailand, the focus must shift to expanding the labor force via women’s participation and private-public pension sharing (e.g., New Zealand’s KiwiSaver).
      • Youth Dividends: In South Asia, the challenge remains creating enough high-quality service jobs to prevent high youth unemployment from becoming a liability.

***

Notable Quotes

"I believe that we at the IMF are like a holder of a flashlight at the time of deep fog surrounding us. And we can only hold this flashlight when we bring our membership together."

— Kristalina Georgieva, Managing Director, IMF

"Communication is the key... truthfully explaining the short-term pain versus long-term benefits. Without that, you can see a lot of unrest in our region."

— Nandalal Weerasinghe, Governor, Central Bank of Sri Lanka

"We are stronger together than we are separate elements. The last one and a half years has taught us fundamentally that greater integration within the region has dividends it can pay."

— Amir Hamzah Azizan, Minister of Finance, Malaysia

"We don’t just give them fish, we try to give them the rod... we need to bring the informal economy to the formal economy using digital finance."

— Ekniti Nitithanprapas, Finance Minister, Thailand

Thank you for joining us for the final session. We know that the previous sessions have been really outstanding, interesting, and really thought-provoking. Asia has been an economic success story for half a century now. Billions of people have been lifted out of poverty, and Asia has indeed reshaped the global economy. But the path to 2050 will be different from the path to 2000. And that's because we're navigating a completely different environment right now. We have higher debt, fiscal conditions are tighter, we have increasing geopolitical tensions, and supply chains are fragmenting. We have aging populations in some while youth bulges in others. And all this is happening amidst a technological revolution. So the question really is: what policies should be adopted by governments through 2050 to ensure that growth is not just strong but also sustainable and inclusive? And it is my privilege to introduce our panel of speakers today. We have Kristalina Georgieva, Managing Director of the IMF; Ekniti Nitithanprapas, Deputy Prime Minister and Finance Minister of Thailand; Amir Hamzah Azizan, Minister of Finance of Malaysia; and Nandalal Weerasinghe, Governor of the Central Bank of Sri Lanka. Thank you so much for joining us and of course we've tapped the sharpest of minds for this particular panel. Kristalina, let's start with you. In your view, how do you see Asia evolving between now and 2050? You know, taking into consideration all the issues like demographics, climate, and technological innovation. Well, the story of Asia over the last decades, as you said, is very impressive. And it is important to remember why Asia did so well. And Asia did so well from the perspective of where we sit at the Fund because it has done three things. One, it has built a very strong and sound foundation for growth. When we look at the institutions in the majority of countries in Asia, Central Banks, and Finance Ministries, they have adopted sound policies. They have done well to draw lessons from the shocks they have gone through. And I very firmly believe that this is going to work well for Asia going into the future, to have strong institutions that are able to navigate this rapidly changing world. Secondly, Asia has done very well because of the innovation and creativity that has gone in economies in Asia, driving growth, driving solutions to problems they—countries face. And three, Asia has done very well because societies have come together at a time of shocks. I would never forget going to South Korea during the East Asia crisis and talking to people how they are coping with the crisis. They tell me how women would give their jewelry to the treasury to help Korea move through the crisis. And that sense of unity in society is something that I cherish. I see it in Thailand. It is such a tremendous asset. So these are things that Asia will continue to—to have. And then the question is: what are countries facing as their biggest challenges? Demographics, for sure. Part of Asia is aging very rapidly. Part of Asia still has youthful population. But when you look at the countries where populations are growing, youth unemployment is very high. So clearly there has to be focus in the aging Asia on expanding the labor force. Japan has done quite well to bring more women in the labor force. Countries that are aging, they need to think how to make it so that every person that can contribute to the economy does contribute to the economy. How to manage pension liabilities. One thing that is really problematic is how much pension liabilities have gone up. The outlier, not surprisingly, is Japan. It has the population that has been aging the most. And there already today pensions absorb 9 to 10 percent of GDP. How to make it so that there is a better strategy for ensuring dignity and good support for elderly people? Also how to make sure that people work when they are older, if they have the, you know, enthusiasm to do so. I'm one of those. And I think it is really exciting to be able to contribute to—to society. How to create schemes that share the responsibility for pension between the employers and individuals. New Zealand has a KiwiSaver account that does exactly that. And of course in the countries with youthful population, how to create more jobs that would allow young people to be a demographic dividend for their—for their countries. And we know that there are policies that create more jobs. And we see services in Asia being an area where there is a lot that can be done to expand employment. The second big trend, mega-mega trend is technology. Asia has been on the cutting edge of technological advancements. And we see that continuing to be the case. Whether we talk about robotics in Japan or renewable energy sources in China, the ability to come up with solutions is quite—quite impressive. Now we have AI. And for the countries in Asia, AI preparedness, capturing the benefits of AI and managing the risks that come with it, hugely important. We looked into potential of AI and it can lift up productivity and growth in Asia by—in emerging Asia 0.2 to 0.8 percent, in advanced Asia half to one percent. This is significant if you go to the upper end. How to get societies faster adapting to a world of artificial intelligence? And then the third very, very significant mega trend for Asia, it is for the whole world but it is very profound in many countries in Asia, is climate. We see climate shocks hitting countries in Asia with higher frequency and bigger strength. So for Asia, if you are in Bangkok, sea level rise is not a threat of the future; there is also a sense that this is something that—that you have to manage well. So adapting to—to climate change, countries in Asia have been on the forefront. I—again I want to point to Japan. Japan has done remarkably well to have disaster risk reduction strategies in place and to apply them in practice. So managing adaptation to—to climate shocks for Asia is absolutely paramount because if countries fail to do it, the economies are going to—to suffer. I was in Sri Lanka recently visiting an area that was hit by the latest cyclone in end of November. And it is so clear that these kinds of events will continue to come and that if Asia is to be successful in 2050, it has to manage adaptation. And of course it has to be a force for transformation moving to clean energy to green energy. Making sure that if we are to expand energy consumption because of the high appetite for energy that AI has, it has to be clean energy, otherwise Asia and the world would be in trouble. So this time, Sri Lanka went into a very, very difficult situation. So how we resilience is basically thing how we build buffers to be prepared for I think what MD always said several times. So we are able to come out within a relatively short period, compared- thanks to kind of a resilience of the people who have been very patient when we are implementing very, very difficult painful reforms. That's what we learn is that when you implement difficult painful reforms- for example I'll give one example: the first thing in my office in 2022, at the depth of the crisis, first thing I did was to increase policy rate by 700 basis points, from 7.5 percent to 14.5 percent. It's on one day. And next day, we announced inability to service external debt service payments, second day. So these are all, even before we went to the IMF and sought the assistance. So this is a very difficult period. Inflation went to 70 percent, interest rates went to 35 percent, the SMEs also collapsing, but still the ability to communicate truthfully and explaining the short-term pain versus long-term benefits. And communication is the key for us to be able to implement all difficult reforms and go with the people. And without that, you can see lot of unrest in- in our region. If you can see the what- what is happening in Bangladesh recently, the Nepal, and even problems with Pakistan and Maldives and all these countries. So this is important lesson that we learn is that all the difficult reforms, if we do not properly communicate and build credibility into difficult painful reforms, people won't accept. And then you- is a vulnerable situation our part of the country's in, when you- when people don't support the difficult things, there will be huge social unrest. You can't sustain the difficult reforms. So key lesson that we learn is that go with the people and explain the difficulty of implementing the reforms and give them a expectation truthfully and with the credibility. This is the first lesson. And then one is the having institutional strong institutions like the independent Central Bank. It's a key landmark reform that we implemented. Is a root cause of the problem was the fiscal dominance in monetary policy and lack of independent Central Bank. And we brought in that. Second one is fiscal discipline by way of Public Financial Management Act, which established strong fiscal rules and ensure fiscal discipline and consolidation and prevent any kind of unsustainable debt situation going forward. And also other structural reforms. For example, cost recovery pricing for electricity, energy is something that we still politically difficult reforms. But still, with all difficulties people were patient and give us time to implement. Now, within the short period, after only four- three years, inflation was 70 percent was brought to zero percent within 12 months period. The fiscal consolidation from large fiscal primary deficit was 6 percent now over 6 percent surpluses. And from the banking sector, financial sector, huge imbalances, liquidity, capital and foreign exchange crisis situations are in a much better position. So all basically as a result of very committed continuous reforms by administration as well as good support that we got from the- the multilateral organization like the IMF, ADB, World Bank and also other creditors. So these are the lessons I think, key lesson that I would say for any country like in our situation is that identify the root causes of the- the all these issues and address proactively without denial and or without trying to patch up and cover up. No one would be talking about this time of crisis if we had this implemented one year ago. Unfortunately, that political administration did not see that as a issue and trying to patch up with short-term issues without trying to address the root cause of the legacy overall macro imbalances. That is the key fundamental requirement for any country like us to prevent that kind of crisis happening going forward. Kristalina, we keep hearing how governments want to spend, but a lot of these governments are also having high debt. You often talk about high debt, slower growth. When we are in this new world of high uncertainty and more frequent shocks, we need economies to be strong and we need them to grow faster. Unless they do, we cannot address the problem of high debt. Well, first we know that in a rapidly changing environment, private sector is more agile, more adaptable. I wholeheartedly support chop-chop unnecessary regulation, eliminate self-inflicted injuries. Governments have a responsibility to create the conditions for growth, but not to run economic entities. So that attracts capital, that attracts the things along the way, and it provides the base of stability for ASEAN to progress forward. And I think doing more on this side allows ASEAN to compete in a better form as we move forward. If you stack the countries up, we've got countries which are very modern and developed like Singapore, we have also lesser developed countries. So you can't say that Singapore competes with Laos or Cambodia in trade. So why are we fighting? Why don't we look at things that we can help support each other? And if I take one of the recent initiatives that we've been trying to do with Malaysia and Singapore, Johor-Singapore Economic Zone, is a recognition of like, look, we compete better together than we compete trying to pull FDIs in our area. Now what can we do when we zone the areas to be a connected zone? So there's some things that get better done in Malaysia, there's some things that get better done in Singapore, but the combined effort draws capital into that market. And we've seen because we launched our JS-EZ about a year and a bit ago, last year was probably the best year that the state of Johor has actually gotten from a FDI coming in. They ranked the highest growth state from FDI attraction. Why? Because we enable that opportunity to make the addressable market much bigger and we allow both countries to work together. And they found ways to reduce bureaucracy, they found ways to actually co-attract things along the way. I think the more we can grow this inter-country cooperation, both sides get to grow. And from the inter-country cooperation, we grow the inter-ASEAN cooperation, then we get even better and stronger together. A step in the right direction but still not fast enough. We've been talking about this integration for a very long time for ASEAN. Governor Weerasinghe, I'm just wondering how does Sri Lanka look at these regional frameworks? What role do you think Sri Lanka can play? I think the on the regional integration, there was a very good discussion earlier on the ASEAN integration. I think they- they basically what's discussed I certainly agree with that. We talk about even ASEAN talk about this regional integration for long time and it's happening actually, 22 percent is still assessment is that go to 50 percent in 25 years by 2050. But if you compare and discussion was that rather than having the much broad integration, integration in areas where that gives you immediate benefits. For example like discussion was that payment and settlement connections and data sharing, all these easier low-hanging fruits will be much more effective in the short term while moving towards integration. In that context, if you look at the South Asia, it's even much less integrated region. So our inter- inter-regional trade this is less than half of what ASEAN. What is the reason for that? Basically we all within the- within the block, this South Asian block, is very closed protected economies. Although India is a big growing at 5, 6, 7 percent for several years, but if you look at the integration is- I mean this easiest area is for integration when we talk about the minister on the- the energy security. When you look at the capacity of South Asia in terms of renewable energy given this all challenges, Nepal, Bhutan capacity potential several decades has not been utilized. I mean that is the easy one. Why that is not happening? It's kind of internal kind of regulations, restrictions, and fear of opening up for this for investment abroad coming into these areas and then having this investment open and use this potential in those area. Even when it comes to Sri Lanka, we have a huge the wind power, solar power potential. Why can't we have this investment open up and then have a grid connectivity through India to the region? So these are the regional integration easily beneficial and that can lift the region up. And other area is is the digitalization. India has done a great strides in recent past. And why can't- and this is other easy area that we can have more integration in terms of digital connectivity, payment and settlement with India with advanced technologies in terms of digitalization, and our other part of the block and also with connecting with Singapore, Malaysia, other parts of the region. Those are the easy integration rather than talking about much broader. I think political economy what we understand each and every country has difficulties in domestic political realities is not that easy to have much broad integration, but these are the areas people will have immediate benefits and there will be support from the people and it's easier to implement in these areas. So the different governments are taking steps to position themselves for the future through 2050. They're also diversifying the economy. But essentially still, when you take a look at Asia, it's a highly trade-dependent economies. I'm just wondering, Kristalina, how you look at it? I mean, in an increasingly fragmented world, is- is Asia's growth model increasingly at risk? So let me first say that I'm a firm believer that we will continue to trade. And actually if you look at the trade shock from last year, there were many that were predicting that the world would slip into recession. Not the IMF. We said yes, this is not good, but we believe that the resilience of the economies and the role of trade objectively would carry forward. What do we have today? We have countries continuing to rely on trade and- yes, the United States has chosen a particular path from their national perspective, the rest of the world said thank you but no thank you. So I would not discount trade to continue to be an important factor because it does create economies of scale and it provides benefits to trading partners. This being said, I also would stress that there are two trends that in Asia need to be embraced and accelerated. The first one is less reliance on goods, more reliance on services. And actually moving the economies more towards services is a logical next step. So you have from agriculture to manufacturing then from manufacturing to services. And we see in parts of Asia this transition is not as dynamic as it can be. That applies to pretty much almost all economies in the region. The second one is for those countries that have grown based on trade, but have high potential of domestic consumption, turn your eyes more inward. And of course the most obvious case is China. China is now just simply too big to rely on goods exports as their engine for growth. And we have been for quite some time advocating that China needs to turn more towards domestic consumption. I mean for God's sake 1.4 billion consumers, make better use of that engine for your- for your growth. And I'm very pleased to see that recent announcements go in that- in that direction. It is very important for Asia to think about the future also from the perspective of how capital gets allocated in Asia. Still there is a higher role of banks and loans in allocating capital. And we know that banks tend to be more conservative, they tend to not take as much risk, whereas capital markets, equity investment, it allows to accelerate the allocation of resources to the highest impact. So moving towards capital markets more and thinking about Asia's capital markets a bit more as a region rather than each country for its own, I think would go a long way to bring more dynamism and to accelerate growth. Risk appetite in this world of rapid change is paramount. If you don't have venture capital, if you don't have equity investments that take risk, you cannot succeed. And the other thing that I would love to see across Asia is much more ambition in how you help countries that fail to exit and for this to be not a source of shame but a source of pride. I think that solvency regimes still leave some room for improvement. So there are things that can be done that can unleash more innovation and stimulate more dynamism. So if you look at the world and actually we- this is what we would be talking about during our annual meetings. There are two big tasks. One, dynamism and- and stronger growth. I agree that macroeconomic and financial stability is a necessary but not sufficient condition. It is good when it is a foundation for growth. And two, resilience. Resilience to the shocks that would continue to come. And when we are here in October, we want our membership to look into the future with an eye on what have we learned that can be applied to have more dynamic and more resilient world economy. Well national economy, regional economy, world economy. And I for one put my trust in Thailand to create the right environment with the hospitality of the Thai people, with the welcoming they- they would offer, that we would have a conversation with lasting impact for how we go about our future. And that's the perfect way to open the discussion to the floor. If there are any questions, please do raise your hand. Someone will come along with a microphone.
