Transparency, Trade and Fighting Corruption Across the Globe - Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala

Association of Certified Fraud Examiners (ACFE)(12.4K) ·19:48 ·12 views · 2026-03-06
Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala (Director-General, World Trade Organization)
Dr.
TL;DR Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, Director-General of the WTO, asserts that institutional transparency and the strategic use of technology are the most effective deterrents to global corruption, emphasizing that the fight against fraud requires both strong systemic frameworks and...

TL;DR

Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, Director-General of the WTO, asserts that institutional transparency and the strategic use of technology are the most effective deterrents to global corruption, emphasizing that the fight against fraud requires both strong systemic frameworks and unwavering personal integrity.

The Intersection of Global Trade and Anti-Corruption

  • Transparency as a Deterrent: Dr. Okonjo-Iweala highlights that the World Trade Organization’s mission of lifting living standards relies on a body of rules that mandate transparency, stability, and predictability.
      • The "Daylight" Effect: Because fraud and corruption thrive in secrecy, the WTO’s requirement for members to report trade policies acts as a structural barrier to illicit activities.
      • MFN Terms: The "most favored nation" arrangements ensure similar trading terms for all members, reducing the discretionary "backroom deals" that foster corruption.
  • Institutionalizing Openness: While current systems are not perfect, ongoing WTO reforms aim to strengthen notification rules to ensure trade policies remain visible to all stakeholders.

Technology and the Human Element in Fraud Prevention

  • Digitizing High-Risk Processes: Implementing technology in sectors prone to corruption, such as ports and customs, removes the human interaction that often leads to bribery and fraud.
      • Trade Facilitation Agreement: Global implementation of this agreement has lowered fraud by automating inspections and documentation.
  • Balancing Automation and Judgment: While technology should be used to streamline processes that don't require human intervention, Dr. Okonjo-Iweala maintains that human judgment remains indispensable for complex oversight.
  • Reducing Discretionary Power: By moving decision-making out of the hands of individuals and into digitized systems, organizations can significantly reduce the "demand" side of corrupt money.

Strengthening Global and Regional Governance

  • Institutional Weakness vs. Implementation: Success in fighting corruption requires both the existence of strong institutions (judiciaries, anti-corruption agencies) and the political will to implement their mandates.
      • The Need for Consequences: Without visible penalties for wrongdoing, the incentive to engage in fraud remains high.
  • Universal Vigilance: Developed nations must not become complacent. Dr. Okonjo-Iweala warns that even "rich" nations must remain vigilant, as their institutions can be undermined if not constantly protected.
  • Africa’s Rising Role: With Africa projected to have the world’s largest reservoir of young workers by 2050, the continent’s future depends on strengthening internal governance and leveraging the talent of youth in AI and Fintech to bypass legacy corruption issues.

Negotiation Strategies and Leadership Lessons

  • Fact-Based Negotiation: Effective consensus-building requires a foundation of irrefutable facts, though leaders must be prepared for political actors who may ignore data or attack the messenger.
      • Identifying Incentives: Successful negotiators must identify what the opposing party stands to gain or lose to bring them to the table.
  • Lessons for Women in Leadership: Based on her research and career, Dr. Okonjo-Iweala identifies key traits for success:
      • Subject Mastery: Women are often held to different expectations; mastering the subject matter is the best way to command a room.
      • The 70% Rule: Personal commitment is essential for high-stress roles. If a leader does not feel motivated to go to work 70% of the time, the role is likely a poor fit.
      • Moral Compass: Integrity is not just a government requirement but an individual responsibility that must be modeled from the top down.
"Fraud and corruption does not like transparency and openness... When you're transparent and you're open, then it's much more difficult for fraud to take place." — Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala
"If there are no consequences, then the incentive to continue to do the wrong thing abounds. So that's what I think in a lot of our developing countries we need... we need consequences when things happen." — Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala
"Avoiding fraud and corruption also starts from the top. If from the top the example is not there and citizens see that leaders can get away with doing the wrong thing, then why wouldn't they do the wrong thing themselves?" — Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala
"If I don't get out of bed 70% of the time feeling like I want to go to work that day, then I know that job isn't working for me." — Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala
Hello and welcome to Fraud Talk, the ACFE's monthly podcast. I'm John Duffy, and in this episode, we're taking a trip back in time to the 36th annual ACFE Global Fraud Conference. In June 2025, over 5,000 conference attendees had the pleasure of hearing from Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, the Director General of the World Trade Organization, who received the Cressy Award for Lifetime Achievement in Fraud Prevention and Detection. Dr. Okonjo-Iweala is one of the most important figures of the modern day in the fight against fraud and corruption. During her career of more than 40 years, she became the first woman and the first African to be named Director General of the WTO. She was also the first woman to serve as Nigeria's foreign affairs and finance minister, where she oversaw reforms to fight corruption, and she negotiated a write-off of the country's national debt, providing a much-needed financial reboot. She spent 25 years at the World Bank and today serves on the Board of Trustees of the World Economic Forum. Time magazine twice named her one of the 100 most influential people in the world, and Forbes named her one of the world's top 100 most powerful women eight times. During her session, Dr. Okonjo-Iweala explores several themes that you'll hear in this episode: the direct line between integrity, transparency, and accountability; balancing how technology works in tandem with tasks that still require human judgment; and the importance of developing strong institutions and frameworks for preventing and detecting fraud. She also discusses the role that governance and oversight will play in Africa in the coming years and learned lessons for women in leadership. I hope you enjoy this episode, and now, here's a discussion between ACFE Chief Training Officer Andi McNeal and Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala. Thank you so much for taking the time to come sit with us, share your expertise and experiences with us today. I'd love to start our conversation by drawing a direct line between the vital work you do every day at the WTO and the vital work our audience does every day. The mission of the World Trade Organization is to improve the welfare of people around the world by ensuring that trade flows as smoothly, predictably, and freely as possible. How do you see the relationship between that mission of promoting fair and transparent trade and the mission that we are all dedicated to of fighting systemic issues of fraud and corruption? Well, thank you, Andi. I think the World Trade Organization's mission, as you said, is enhancing living standards, helping create employment, and supporting sustainable development. Many people don't know it, so thanks for actually looking into that. So it's lifting up people and their living standards. But it does so through a body of rules that are transparent and ask for transparency, support stability and predictability in world trade through arrangements that offer members, you know, terms. We call it "most favored nation" terms, which are similar terms on which they trade with each other on tariffs and other issues. And I think maintaining transparency in trade, openness in trade, predictability and stability is, in a way, the opposite of fraud and corruption. Fraud and corruption does not like transparency and openness. And so that's what we are about. So there's a link between... well, put it this way, it's the opposite. When you're transparent and you're open, then it's much more difficult for fraud to take place. And this is why it is very important we have our members supposed to report all their trade policies, to notify. And when that does not work well, that's when challenges begin because you don't have the transparency that you need. So the big word here is transparency. What we try to do is bring daylight to the way that trade works. Is it always perfect? The answer is no, we're struggling a bit now. We have to reform to make sure we reintroduce and strengthen all the rules on transparency. So. Well, and I think a lot of us here know the importance of that transparency, not just at the level that you're working at it for, but at our organizations, in our leadership, and even in our teams, right? We recognize the importance of how transparency and frankly integrity, like you spoke about earlier, go hand in hand in a lot of ways. Yeah, and can I just add that one of the... I really believe in technology because I believe that, you know, when you can streamline, digitize, and use technology to really govern a lot of or institutionalize a lot of your processes, it helps because it takes a lot out of the hands of people. So I'm a big fan of technology, not unbridled technology, but technology that makes a difference in terms of allowing things to move faster, quicker, and taking so much decision-making that can lead to people asking for things out of the hands of people. Well, I think that's a balance we all need to strike. I'm curious, from what you just said, where is that balance between human judgment and making sure that we are showing up and ensuring that transparency and allowing the technology to help us do that? Well, you can't do away with human judgment. There's no way you can do that. But you know you start where there are processes that actually don't really need that kind of judgment and intervention. And that's where you can digitize those processes. A lot of work on trade facilitation, for instance, involves filling all kinds of documents, inspections, and so on. Those allow an element of human interaction that can lead to fraud and corruption. But when you put technology in and digitize, it takes away the need for all that. Processes become faster at ports and customs, and the human element is held back. You know, in every country, ports and customs is a place where there is enormous fraud and corruption worldwide. And so implementing our trade facilitation agreement has really helped to lower fraud and corruption. Well, and jumping off on that concept of transparency, so much of your career has been focused on fighting corruption and promoting transparency in government specifically. What do you think the largest challenge we're still facing in combating government corruption is, especially in developing nations, which you spoke a little bit about as well? Well, I think that where institutions are strong and where processes are in place, you tend to have less corruption. In a lot of developing countries where you see institutional weakness—and developed nations, might I add, because remember I spoke about the supply and demand of corrupt money—that is where challenges occur. Where you don't have well-institutionalized processes and strong institutions, a strong judiciary, a strong anti-corruption fighting agency, and strong implementation, problems arise. Because sometimes you can have the laws and the institutions and they're just not implemented; they're ignored. You need both the institutions and you need the implementation. Sometimes you do need to make examples of those who do wrong. If there are no consequences, then the incentive to continue to do the wrong thing abounds. So that's what I think: in a lot of our developing countries we need stronger institutions, a strong and independent judiciary, and strong anti-corruption fighting agencies. We need consequences when things happen. But in developed countries, you also need the same. I don't think anyone today in these days should assume that, "Look, we are good," because we're finding that's not the case. You know, "We're rich, we're developed, we have the institutions, they can never be undermined." Absolutely not. You have to be vigilant to keep those institutions in place. And then I want to talk of the personal. Fighting corruption is also not all about government. It starts with you and your values and your principles and inculcating that. So it's not only a government thing; it's an individual thing. And I think that, to your point, applies in every jurisdiction, regardless of where they are on the development spectrum. I listened to several of your presentations and read transcripts and I've noticed several themes, and one of them I found very inspiring. You've spoken frequently on a theme of a rising, growing, and optimistic Africa, an Africa that's ready to face the challenges ahead of it head-on. We have a very large and growing membership base in Africa and we're so proud of the work that they do every day fighting fraud. What is your perspective on the role of Africa and specifically the certified fraud examiners in Africa as we enter this next stage in the fight against global corruption? Well, first I think some perspective on the continent is needed. I think by 2050 we'll have the third or fourth largest population in the world, and certainly the largest reservoir of young working people. And so this is a continent from all perspectives that everyone needs to pay attention to. It has rising talent. I'm very proud of the younger people because they're really, with respect to AI and fintech—much as we might be left behind—a lot of our young people are doing the right thing. So I think for the future with respect to fraud and corruption, for us to really realize our potential, we need a lot of internal work. We need work on strengthening governance within our governments. We really do. We have to strengthen our institutions like I talked about and ensure a right body of laws, implementation of those laws, and a strong judiciary, for instance. We need the governance environment and we need to lead by example. You know, avoiding fraud and corruption also starts from the top. If from the top the example is not there and citizens see that leaders can get away with doing the wrong thing, then why wouldn't they do the wrong thing themselves? So we need the leadership, the strength of character, the values and principles, and the institutions. We need to use technology to help us solve a lot of these problems that lead to fraud and corruption. I really believe in that, and we need people like you. We do, absolutely. And I think that's a great message for all of us to take away, but I know we have so many CFEs in Africa watching today and really inspired by your experiences and everything you're sharing. So thank you for that. Another area that I think you could probably provide helpful perspective to our audience: you're widely known as a skilled negotiator and a strong consensus builder. Especially as we're working to strengthen those institutions and build improved governance structures, our members often find themselves having to convince people to hear messages they don't want to hear. They must gain buy-in when there's a wide variety of perspectives in a room. Can you share an example of when you had a particularly challenging time building consensus and what lessons you learned from that that maybe our audience can apply if they're in a similarly difficult situation? Well, I find that in negotiating, when you're dealing rationally, you need facts. What has helped me truly in all these situations is having all the facts that can inform the negotiations—facts that people cannot run away from, that you cannot invent. I've found that when you put facts in front of people, it sometimes works. But not always. You know, I've also in trade, coming from a finance to a trade background, found that when it's political, people just ignore the facts. Oh, we never hear that. They ignore the facts or they blame the facts. Or if they can't blame the facts, they blame the messenger. So I think what I try to do is to point to the consequences of bad action in the negotiation. And third, to look for what it is that the people you're negotiating with would benefit from in the situation. I think you have to look; if people don't see any benefit to themselves in the outcomes of a negotiation, they're not likely to go there. So even under the worst circumstances, trying to build consensus and negotiate and have a result, you have to look at: what will this party gain that you can persuade them to? What will they lose if they're not gaining? They might lose something that you can use as an incentive to bring them to the table. What are the facts, even if they're ignoring the facts? Or if they don't want to accept the facts, what are the consequences of their action? So it's a whole bundle. And of course, you have to create sometimes a rapport with those you're negotiating with. Being in a conflictual situation doesn't help. So some empathy building and trying to understand their situation and where they're coming from always helps. Well, and so much of what you've said actually echoes what I know we all bring to interviews, right? That's a lot of the same perspective we bring when we're trying to have someone share something they did that maybe wasn't the right thing to do. But it's very interesting to kind of flip that and think of it through the lens of: this isn't the same type of situation, but I still need to work through that lens of how can this benefit you to share this, or what might be the consequences of if we don't reach a consensus here. So that's really interesting. This next question, please address it to the audience, but I'm going to pretend you're talking directly to me. You've mentioned how important it is for women leaders to learn from those who have come before them. So I have a two-part question for you. First, what lessons have you taken from women leaders that you've looked up to? And second, what advice and inspiration do you hope to leave for the future women leaders that are watching you and listening to you today? Well, thank you. I think from women leaders that I've interacted with, I've learned a lot about having courage. Courage because they're often put in situations that are very difficult and impossible—situations in which it is easier to run away from what you know is the truth rather than stick up for it. So courage. I think values and principles, the same things I referred to, are vital. These women have strong values and strong principles and they stick to them. I've learned that that helps. You have to have some moral compass. This is not the audience to say that to because you already do, but if you don't have your own strong moral compass, it's very easy to go wrong. I'm sorry, it's very easy to go wrong and it's also difficult to guide others. So I learned that—sticking to my principles, values, and moral compass. And to a large extent, you have to also be empathetic. And this applies to all leaders, not just women. If you don't have that ability to absorb the atmosphere and the people you're dealing with, you can easily also go wrong. So these are the things: values, principles. And I want to say one more thing: working hard. You know, the women—all the women—and I've written a book also on that. It's called "Women and Leadership: Real Lives, Real Lessons" with Julia Gillard, something of a bestseller. The only bestseller I've written. But it interviews eight women and, you know, all of them have very similar lessons from very different backgrounds. It is mastering your subject and the ability to work hard. Because no matter how we slice and dice it, when women are leaders, expectations of them differ once they walk into a room from men. And all these women, from Jacinda Ardern to Christine Lagarde, to Hillary Clinton, to Ellen Johnson Sirleaf, all of them found had similar experiences. So if you master your subject, as they did, and work incredibly hard, as they did, these are some of the things I also took away. And I share them with others. Yeah, but can I... one thing I do want to share from my own side is any work you choose to do as you move up the ladder and when you're in leadership, please make sure you have some fun. I have not... or if you don't have fun, that you're really committed. If you're working hard—I work incredible hours, 12, 14 hours every day—either it has to be fun even if it's very serious work, or there has to be enormous commitment to it. Without those, it just becomes a drudge. I often share—I'm sure you've heard it in the things I release—that if I don't get out of bed 70% of the time feeling like I want to go to work that day, then I know that job isn't working for me. I love that. Well, I hope you enjoyed this flashback to the ACFE Global Fraud Conference and I really hope you'll join us at the 37th annual ACFE Global Fraud Conference being held in Boston, Massachusetts and virtually July 12th to the 17th, 2026. You can find every episode of Fraud Talk on ACFE.com, Apple Podcasts, Spotify, YouTube, or wherever you listen. I'm John Duffy, signing off.

TL;DR

President Trump signaled a direct role in selecting Iran’s future leadership and claimed Cuba is nearing a "fall" due to U.S. pressure, while the administration manages a significant Cabinet shift with Kristi Noem’s departure from DHS.

Foreign Policy: Iran, Cuba, and Venezuela

  • Iran Succession Strategy: Following U.S. strikes against Iranian leadership, Trump stated the U.S. is actively looking at potential successors.
      • The Heir Apparent: Trump noted the regime is considering the son of the Ayatollah, but claimed the father is hesitant because the son is viewed as "incompetent."
      • U.S. Intervention: Trump insisted the U.S. will be involved in the transition to ensure a "proper choice" is made to avoid recurring conflict.
  • Cuba on the Brink: Trump asserted that Cuba is "on the brink" of collapse because his administration’s intervention in Venezuela cut off the island's oil supply.
      • Diplomatic Channels: He confirmed the U.S. is currently in talks with the Cuban regime.
      • Rubio’s Role: Secretary of State Marco Rubio is reportedly lead on the Cuba file.
  • The Venezuela Parallel: Trump praised Venezuelan official Delcy Rodriguez as "fantastic" and indicated he is seeking a similar figure to work with in Iran.

Cabinet Shakeup: DHS Transition

  • Kristi Noem’s Exit: Noem is leaving her post as Secretary of Homeland Security (DHS) but will remain within the administration in a different capacity.
      • Rare Reassignment: Analysts noted Trump is typically "loath to surrender to bad headlines," but a combination of events in Minnesota and recent hearings "sealed her fate."
  • Succession: Markwayn Mullin is expected to step into the DHS Secretary role.
  • Internal Policy Tensions: The department remains caught between two factions:
      • Hardliners: Led by Stephen Miller, pushing for aggressive deportations.
      • Moderates: Those concerned with the economic impact of labor shortages in hospitality and agriculture, with Trump himself suggesting a "softer" approach might be necessary in some regions.

Global Export of American Political Strategy

  • Transatlantic Consulting: Morris Kats, a top advisor to New York progressive Zohran Mamdani, recently traveled to the United Kingdom to advise the Labour and Green parties.
  • The "Progressive Template": There is a surging appetite in Europe for the political styles of progressives like Mamdani and Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez as a counter-measure to rising right-wing populism.
  • American Dominance: Veteran consultant James Carville noted a global perception that American consultants are "better than they actually are," leading to frequent "exports" of U.S. campaign tactics to countries like Albania and the UK.

Vast CEO Max Haot on raising $500M in new investment round

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·11:43 ·180 views · 2026-03-06
Max Haot (CEO, Vast) Morgan Brennan (Anchor, CNBC)
Vast has secured $500M in its first major outside funding round to accelerate the development of Haven-1, a private space station set to launch in Q1 2025 as a primary contender to replace the International Space Station (ISS).
TL;DR Vast has secured $500M in its first major outside funding round to accelerate the development of Haven-1, a private space station set to launch in Q1 2025 as a primary contender to replace the International Space Station (ISS). Funding Milestone: Vast announced a $500M...

TL;DR

Vast has secured $500M in its first major outside funding round to accelerate the development of Haven-1, a private space station set to launch in Q1 2025 as a primary contender to replace the International Space Station (ISS).

Capital Raise and Strategic Positioning

  • Funding Milestone: Vast announced a $500M investment round, marking its first significant outside capital. This follows over $900M in previous funding from founder Jed McCaleb, bringing total investment to more than $1.4 billion.
  • The "Leapfrog" Strategy: CEO Max Haot described a strategy of building a "minimum viable product" station (Haven-1) using internal funds rather than waiting for NASA development grants.
      • By launching hardware ahead of the competition, Vast aims to become "impossible to ignore" during NASA’s upcoming Commercial LEO Destination (CLD) Phase 2 contract awards.
  • ISS Replacement Timeline: The company is laser-focused on the 2030 (or potentially 2032) retirement of the ISS, aiming to provide a seamless commercial transition for the U.S. and allied partners.

Product Roadmap and Launch Timeline

  • Haven-1 (Q1 2025): A single-module space station launching on a SpaceX Falcon 9.
      • Initial operations will be remote-controlled to verify systems.
      • Following verification, a four-person crew will visit the station via a SpaceX Dragon spacecraft.
  • Haven-2: The long-term successor, a four-module station designed for mass production, capable of supporting continuous human habitation and significant power/life support requirements.
  • Haven Demo Success: The company recently completed its "Haven Demo" mission, a satellite test of non-human technologies (power, solar arrays, propulsion, and flight computers), which Haot characterized as a "100% mission success."
  • SpaceX Integration: Vast is the first station developer to integrate Starlink laser terminals, enabling gigabit connectivity for research and crew communications.

Market Outlook and Business Model

  • Target Customers: Vast is prioritizing "sovereign nation" customers—specifically NASA, ESA (Europe), and JAXA (Japan)—who wish to send astronauts for research and science.
  • Conservative Economic Approach: Unlike competitors relying on a near-term "LEO Economy" (space manufacturing or tourism), Vast’s business model assumes profitability based on existing government demand.
      • In-space Manufacturing: While bullish on pharmaceuticals and semiconductors long-term (5–10 years), Haot stated Vast does not require these markets to be viable in the immediate future.
  • Space Data Centers: While open to the technology due to high power requirements, Haot views space-based data centers as more suited to high-power satellites than human habitation modules.

Government and Competitive Landscape

  • NASA CLD Program: Congress and NASA are moving toward issuing an RFP for the second phase of commercial space station development.
  • Need for Redundancy: Haot urged NASA to select at least two winners for the replacement program to ensure redundancy and backup for the U.S. and its partners.
  • Operational Training: Vast was recently selected for the 6th private astronaut mission to the ISS (launching July 2025). This mission serves as a "stepping stone" to gain operational experience with NASA and SpaceX before launching their own crewed missions to Haven-1.

Notable Quotes

"If we do all of that [launch Haven-1 and return a crew safely]... I think we'll be impossible to ignore for NASA in terms of the hardware that we have." — Max Haot, CEO
"We believe that a provider should become profitable on the current market. And the current market is simply international space agencies... We are not dependent on [the LEO economy] becoming a few hundred million dollar revenue per year in two years." — Max Haot, CEO
"We think it [the ISS] should be retired when there is a commercial replacement available, and we are ready to do that." — Max Haot, CEO
Joining me now is Max Haot, the CEO of Vast. And Max, it is great to speak with you today. You have just announced that you've raised $500 million. This is the first significant significant outside investment round for Vast. What does it enable? So for us, you know, it's continuing our roadmap of building America's next space station and also for allied partners of the United States. You know, we are laser focused on having the privilege to replace the International Space Station. So that means both building all the technology and infrastructure that we need to do it. We are well on the way with it. We will be launching our first single module space station, Haven-1, in Q1 next year, and you can see a model of it right here and also some of the hardware behind me. And then NASA is running a competition called the Commercial LEO Destination competition this year. Congress is obviously pushing to have a decision so that, you know, the commercial providers can have a replacement to the International Space Station before it is retired in 2030, or maybe now there are discussions to 2032. So funding our operation and our growth so that we will be ready and very competitive when bidding and building the replacement to the ISS. Yeah, and you just mentioned it, but just this week lawmakers are moving forward to lay out a timeline to get to this second piece, the second phase of the Commercial Lower Earth Orbit Destinations, or CLD program, to field commercial space stations. So what are your expectations now that we are getting some more detail about how this could go? I mean, I think the two messages in the bill, right? One is NASA must move as quickly as possible and award—issue the RFP and then award it to two or more winners. So we definitely welcome that. At the same time, we are so excited to see what Jared Isaacman has been doing to the Artemis program and the clarity of having stepping stones and increasing cadence. And so, you know, we believe he will announce what will happen to our program as soon as he as he's ready. We are confident it will be it will be it will be good and logical for America and for commercial providers that are building real hardware and delivering, such as us. So the next step is really, you know, issuing the RFP and we are waiting and ready for it. And what's so interesting about Vast versus some of the other commercial space station players is that you haven't already received that NASA money to build out and develop your space station. You've done it all; you funded it all in-house for the most part, right? Yeah, we have a unique situation that we joined the race to replace the International Space Station late in 2023 when NASA had already picked and funded, with another phase one competition, some of our competitors. When we announced that it was our intent to win this competition—the phase two which is due to be awarded this year—we came up with a leapfrog strategy. Which is our thesis, which is: why don't we just build a space station, a minimum viable product space station called Haven-1, launch it, bring a crew to it with a Dragon of four, and come back home safely? And if we do all of that, or on the way to do that, I think we'll be impossible to ignore for NASA in terms of the hardware that we have. And right now you're showing actually a step before that. Before we have crew on our station, we'll actually launch the station without a crew, and before that we launched a satellite which is there to test all of the technologies of the space station that are not human technology. So the power, the solar array you just saw deploy, the propulsion, the flight computer, the ground station, the ground control room, the operators. And so that mission was called Haven Demo, launched in November last year and was just deorbited and was a 100% mission success. The next one is the Haven-1 station, which you can see right there, launching without the crew on a Falcon 9 rocket. We check it out remotely, make sure it's all working, and then we send a crew on a Crew Dragon. So the key step of Vast is stepping stones. We can't just become the ISS in one go, and we have to do it in stepping stones to eventually a four-module that will have enough power and life support system to enable continuous crew. So that's our unique aspect. And it's enabled... you know, before the $500 million round that we announced today, we had investment from our founder, Jed McCaleb, in excess of $900 million. Now we've invested over a billion dollars in American space station technologies, design, infrastructure, and facilities so that we will be ready to replace the International Space Station and build all these modules in mass production before it is retired in 2030, now maybe 2032. We think it should be retired when there is a commercial replacement available, and we are ready to do that. Yeah, and I think that's what's so notable to me about what you just laid out, the stepping stones and the strategy here, but you are moving so quickly to develop all this technology. What is enabling you to do that, especially when you look at other competitors that have taken longer? Well, I think you know in the 1960s, you know, they were going probably faster than we are now with no computer and no tools. And you know, we have also the trailblazing of SpaceX to thank—you know, vertical integration, rapid iteration, and doing it at low cost in a way that remains safe. Clearly, you've seen the Dragon spacecraft that brought back the ability for America to send astronauts, sent more than 60 people, maybe now 70 people to the ISS and other missions. And thanks to that transition to commercial, we are now able to leverage it and now focus on doing the same commercial transition for the destination, the space station. So, you know, it's been done before. You know, it comes from having the resources to do it, the right culture, and the right strategy of iteration and stepping stones. And most important is the incredible team we have been able to attract and hire and motivate, which really, you know, believe in our mission and believe we have what it takes to do it. And without that team, which is an incredible amount of human space flight experience, none of this would be possible. How do you see the market opportunity for commercialized low Earth orbit and for a space station such as Haven-2? Yeah, we we see it quite differently than than what we hear from our competitors. Our view is that the end goal—Vast wants to be the best creator of human habitation in space, right? Including on the moon when these opportunities will come and on Mars and also other orbits, not just low Earth orbit with the ISS replacement. That's our end goal; we want to be the best, obviously safest, lowest cost there. If you look at the opportunity today, we believe that a provider should become profitable on the current market. And the current market is simply international space agencies led by ESA in Europe, the European Space Agency, JAXA in Japan, and obviously NASA as the core, you know, one of the largest customers. And then there are more and more countries around the world that are getting involved in human space flight, especially as the price is reducing thanks to commercial. They want to bring their industry to space, they want to bring their astronauts, they want to do important science and research. So that's today's market, right? Sovereign nations, including America, sending crewed missions to space. There's a little bit of private, but we definitely are not in the space tourism era and that's not really a growing segment, one we focus on. We're sure we will have some of them. And then there is the long-term future, which is the LEO economy: doing in-space manufacturing of drugs, of semiconductors. And so our view is that Vast must be able to be profitable thanks to our low-cost approach and obviously the investment we've put in place on the current market. And we need NASA as a customer, we need Europe as a customer, we need Japan as a customer and others, but we have a plan that allows us to be profitable on that market. And then as we have a station up there and as we are profitable, we will get to unlock the low Earth orbit economy and figure out with pharmaceutical companies and semiconductor companies these new microgravity opportunities to create products. But we are not dependent on that becoming a few hundred million dollar revenue per year in two years. You know, it might take 10 years, it might take five. So that's our conservative approach, which I think is very different than some of our competitors, which I do believe are relying on the arrival of the LEO economy in a few years, and we don't think that that might be realistic. Hmm. It sounds like the competitive landscape is due for a shakeout potentially, if I'm reading between the lines on what you're saying. I mean, you know, we really hope that NASA picks at least two companies in... you know, I wish they could pick more, but the budget is fairly limited, but it should not be one. So I certainly would like, you know, one other competitor at least to be successful. We will be ready for the call to replace the ISS. I believe we will be successful, obviously. And you know, maybe there'll be eventually space for many more. But right now we really hope that two of us will perform and will give the redundancy and the backup that NASA and America and its partners deserve. Of course, you could also award two and only one performs. And so that's why it's really critical for NASA to pick two at the next phase. Are you thinking about this opportunity regarding data centers in space? I mean, we don't think data centers in space actually mix with human space flight. On Haven-1, we have gigabit connectivity from Starlink laser. We are the first space station that SpaceX has agreed to provide laser terminals that go directly to Starlink. So watch this space; we'll figure out whether we play a role in it. In terms of marketing, in terms of working with SpaceX, but more importantly getting to know as a company the International Space Station—the product we are trying to replace and improve—working with NASA on delivering a mission and all of the integration milestones and so on, we think are great stepping stones to have the privilege to replace the International Space Station. Iran War Volatility, Private Credit Fears Grow, & Solar Continues to Crash | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(160.0K) ·19:13 ·64.3K views · 2026-03-06
Steve Eisman (Senior Portfolio Manager, Neuberger Berman)
The narrative has shifted from "all bullish" to "all bearish" as the $1.8 trillion industry faces a liquidity crunch.
TL;DR: Market volatility is currently driven by the escalation of war in Iran and a rapidly deteriorating narrative in private credit, where rising fraud, redemption "gating," and sudden devaluations suggest the first significant credit cycle since 2008. Iran War Response:...

TL;DR: Market volatility is currently driven by the escalation of war in Iran and a rapidly deteriorating narrative in private credit, where rising fraud, redemption "gating," and sudden devaluations suggest the first significant credit cycle since 2008.

Geopolitical Impacts & Macro Volatility

  • Iran War Response: Following U.S. and Israeli strikes on Iran, markets have experienced extreme volatility. While markets initially rallied on the hope of a quick resolution, yields are rising—contrary to typical "flight to safety" behavior—due to fears that high oil prices will trigger renewed inflation.
  • Bitcoin as a Hedge: Eisman notes that Bitcoin failed to act as a non-correlated hedge during the crisis. It dropped as war broke out while gold, silver, and oil spiked; it remains highly correlated with tech stocks.
  • Travel Sector: Airlines and cruise lines saw sharp declines but have shown signs of recovery as investors bet on a decisive U.S. military outcome.

The Private Credit & Private Equity Downturn

The narrative has shifted from "all bullish" to "all bearish" as the $1.8 trillion industry faces a liquidity crunch.

  • Systemic Fraud: A third lending fraud in six months was uncovered at UK-based Market Financial Solutions (MFS) involving "double pledging" of assets. Major firms exposed include Barclays, Apollo, Jeffries, and Wells Fargo.
  • Redemption Pressures:
      • Invico Capital: Implementing "structured liquidity management" (gating) to handle large institutional redemption requests.
      • Blackstone (B-Credit): Hit with 7.9% redemption requests (exceeding its 5% limit). Blackstone used $400 million of company and employee capital to cover the gap to avoid the negative optics of gating the fund.
  • Abrupt Devaluations: BlackRock’s public BDC (TCPC) marked a $25 million loan to an Amazon aggregator to zero, despite valuing it at par (100 cents) just three months ago.
  • Equity Performance: Year-to-date, the private equity group has been "obliterated," with Apollo, Blackstone, and KKR all down 24–26%, and Blue Owl down 31%.

Sector Crises: Solar, Health Insurance, and Software

  • Solar Sector Collapse: The "bottom is not yet in" for solar.
      • Sunrun shares dropped 35% following disastrous results.
      • Residential solar is struggling with the loss of federal subsidies and high interest rates, while First Solar is being pressured by the current administration's anti-renewable policies.
  • Elevance "Excommunication": The health insurer faced severe CMS sanctions, suspending enrollment in its Medicare Advantage plans.
      • Data Negligence: CMS alleged Elevance failed to submit proper data corrections for years, repeatedly sending data on USB flash drives that CMS could not open.
      • Financial Risk: The company faces potential "retro clawback" payments and loss of revenue bonuses, sending the stock down 17% YTD.
  • Software & AI Layoffs:
      • MongoDB fell 23% after issuing weak guidance.
      • Block (formerly Square) is laying off 4,000 employees (40% of its workforce). The CEO attributed the move to AI-driven efficiencies, raising concerns about a broader trend of "white-collar layoffs."

Institutional Risks in Life Insurance

  • Private Equity Integration: PE firms are increasingly buying life insurance companies to use their "long duration liabilities" to fund private credit loans.
  • Hidden Leverage: Analyst Tom Gober warns that these firms are using offshore captive reinsurance companies to move liabilities overseas, effectively hiding increased leverage and increasing systemic risk within the life insurance sector.
"The fun starts to go out of these high-paying jobs when your own money is lined up to backstop redemptions." — Steve Eisman on Blackstone using employee capital to cover withdrawals.
"Getting in was easy, but getting out is a whole different story." — Lakshmi Ganapathy (quoted by Eisman) on the current liquidity trap in private credit.
"In the banking world, [these sanctions are] like excommunication. I think Elevance just got excommunicated." — Steve Eisman
The big news of the week, of course, started over the weekend with the United States and Israel bombing Iran. Once again, Bitcoin in a crisis correlates with tech stocks and does not act as a hedge. The news keeps getting worse in private credit land. A fraud was uncovered. This is the third lending fraud that has been exposed during the last six months. Block is laying off 4,000 employees, which is 40% of its workforce. Is this the start of massive white-collar layoffs, or is it company-specific? I don't know yet, but it is worrisome. Hi, this is Steve Eisman and welcome to another edition of the Weekly Wrap. This is for the week ending Friday, March 6, 2026, but recorded Thursday night, March 5. Before we start, I want to point out that I am on Professor Scott Galloway's podcast called The Prof G Pod that drops today, this Friday. And now for the wrap. This week's wrap is about the power of a narrative in new and ongoing situations. In an odd way, despite a war, the rest of the week had very little news, so I will explore the ongoing situations. I'm going to cover the following: One, war in Iran and the investment implications. Two, more bad news in private credit/private equity land. Three, more disaster in the solar sector. So let's get started. The big news of the week, of course, started over the weekend with the United States and Israel bombing Iran and killing Ayatollah Khamenei and much of Iran's top leadership. I would just point out that once again, Bitcoin did not act as a hedge for uncertainty. Over the weekend, as the war raged, oil, gold, and silver were all up a lot, and Bitcoin went down. By Monday, the market opened down and then rallied back. NASDAQ ended Monday slightly up and Bitcoin went up with it all day as usual. Once again, Bitcoin in a crisis correlates with tech stocks and does not act as a hedge. In addition, and almost needless to say, all travel stocks in reaction to the news of the crisis went down on Monday, from airlines to cruise lines to travel booking companies. Two things remain true: the correlation between Bitcoin and tech stocks remains very high, and retail investors continue to buy every single dip. I'd also point out that on Tuesday, the market corrected again as the realization grew that this war will take more than just a few days. Now, usually in times of uncertainty, yields on US treasuries go down as investors seek safety and buy treasury bonds. Not this time. And on Thursday, the market went back down. All the volatility is due to investors trying to figure out the implications of the Iran war. And I imagine this volatility will continue for weeks. Moving on. The news keeps getting worse in private credit land. Four pieces of bad news. First, a fraud was uncovered. A UK-based private lender called Market Financial Solutions, with a loan book of 2.4 billion, is being liquidated, and it looks like there is double pledging of assets. Some major firms are exposed including Barclays, Apollo, Jeffries, and Wells Fargo. While MFS is not a big lender, this is the third lending fraud that has been exposed during the last six months. The other two being Tricolor and First Brands. Once again, a lack of adequate due diligence allowed fraud to grow unhindered. Double pledging assets is discoverable, or it would be if the private asset lenders were more careful. Second piece of news: another relatively small private credit manager, Invico Capital—at least that's how I think it's pronounced—that manages around 3 billion, has been receiving large redemption requests for one of its funds from its institutional investors. To deal with these redemption requests, Invico adopted what is called a structured liquidity management plan. Now, what that exactly is was unclear from press reports, but it sounds like some form of gating where Invico restricts redemptions as it tries to wind down the fund. So now it looks like it is not only retail investors who are getting fearful of private credit, but institutional investors are worrying as well. Because of this, on Friday of last week, the entire financial sector suffered a correction, and it continued to act badly this week as people have more and more reason to worry about a credit cycle. These financial stock price declines represent fears that a credit cycle is unfolding for the first time since the Great Financial Crisis. The irony is that the banks just reported a few weeks ago, and the conclusion from those results was that credit quality is fine. And here we are, less than a month later, and it's not fine. Or at least there are fears that it is not fine. This is an example of how a narrative can change very quickly. Third piece of news occurred this week: Blackstone got hit. Blackstone's 82 billion private credit fund called B-Credit got hit with redemptions amounting to 7.9% of the fund, but the fund only allows 5% quarterly redemptions. Blackstone paid out the entire redemption partially by increasing the limit of quarterly withdrawals from 5% to 7% and using company and employee capital to cover the remaining 400 million of redemption requests. Now you know things are dicey when Blackstone is afraid of being compared to Blue Owl and chooses self-funding over gating. The fun starts to go out of these high-paying jobs when your own money is lined up to backstop redemptions. And the fourth piece of bad news occurred on Thursday. BlackRock has a small public BDC called BlackRock TCP Capital. The symbol is TCPC. Now, BDCs are companies that make loans and many BDCs make loans that fund acquisitions by private equity companies. It marked that loan from 25 million, which was par, to zero. Worthless. Three months ago, TCPC valued that same loan at 100 cents on the dollar. To sum up the narrative about private credit, I'll quote Lakshmi Ganapathy of Unicus Research, a guest on our show many times: "Getting in was easy but getting out is a whole different story." Now, what does that mean? Well, here's what happened: Elevance got hit with sanctions across all of their Medicare Advantage plans that would stop all Medicare Advantage enrollment and implement member communication restrictions. It's related to risk adjustment data submissions. CMS alleged non-compliance in how Elevance submitted their data from 2015 through 2023. Basically, Elevance was supposed to submit any possible data corrections to CMS's website, but Elevance kept sending them a USB flash drive instead. CMS could not open the flash drive, so it was worthless, and they kept warning Elevance each year. So CMS had enough and is pinning potential sanctions on Elevance. Also, Elevance never reimbursed any potential overpayment. They just kept all the money during that entire period. There could be a big retro clawback payment and if sanctions happen there could also be big impacts to membership and star ratings. Star ratings are important for Medicare Advantage plans, out of five stars, updated once annually. If you score four stars or higher, you get a 5% revenue bonus. Elevance's stock was down 8% on this news this week and is down 17% this year. A good question. Now, my view of the Tom Gober interview is that he raised two very important issues. First, private equity has purchased life insurance companies because life insurance companies have long duration liabilities. Private equity companies want to place their long duration loans into life insurance companies because of the matching duration of assets and liabilities. And that actually makes sense. Tom's worry is that these private credit loans are too risky. Now, he might be right, but thus far there has not been any major credit issue within these life insurance loan books. Problems may emerge, but they have not yet. Through that convoluted process, their leverage has increased and is also hidden. Bessent says LARGEST bombing campaign on Iran happens ‘tonight’

Fox Business(3.3M) ·12:15 ·230.0K views · 2026-03-06
Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary)
The U.S.
TL;DR The U.S. Treasury and the DFC have launched a $20 billion maritime reinsurance program to bypass private insurance withdrawals and restore shipping through the Strait of Hormuz, coinciding with a massive military strike tonight aimed at degrading Iranian infrastructure to...

TL;DR

The U.S. Treasury and the DFC have launched a $20 billion maritime reinsurance program to bypass private insurance withdrawals and restore shipping through the Strait of Hormuz, coinciding with a massive military strike tonight aimed at degrading Iranian infrastructure to stabilize global energy markets.

Maritime Reinsurance and Shipping Restoration

  • $20 Billion Reinsurance Plan: Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent announced that the International Development Finance Corporation (DFC) will provide up to $20 billion in ship finance and war risk insurance.
      • Addressing the Insurance Gap: The plan responds to private insurers (such as Lloyd's of London) dropping coverage for vessels in the Gulf following the start of the conflict.
      • Coverage Scope: Reinsurance will cover ships transporting oil, refined products, and fertilizer.
      • Limited Risk Window: Bessent noted that DFC coverage is only required while ships are within the Gulf and the Strait; once in open waters, vessels can revert to standard private insurance.
  • Securing the Strait of Hormuz: The administration is coordinating with CENTCOM to provide naval escorts for tankers if necessary.
      • Timeline for Resolution: Bessent expects the shipping bottleneck to be resolved within one to two weeks as confidence returns to the maritime industry.
  • Rebuttal of Financial Criticism: Bessent dismissed reports from *JP Morgan* and the *Financial Times* regarding DFC capital constraints, calling their analysis "irresponsible" and "low grade."

Energy Market Strategy and Price Stabilization

  • Targeting Lower Oil Prices: The administration expects oil to return to the $55–$60 per barrel range once shipping resumes and domestic production continues to scale.
      • Current U.S. Production: Larry Kudlow noted the U.S. is producing 13.6 million barrels of oil per day and 24 million barrels of total liquid fuels—more than Russia and Saudi Arabia combined.
  • Strategic Supply Management: Treasury is actively managing global supply to prevent price spikes during the conflict.
      • India/Russia Waiver: Treasury has permitted India to accept Russian oil already "on the water" to bridge temporary supply gaps.
      • Unsanctioning Barrels: Bessent indicated that Treasury may "unsanction" hundreds of millions of barrels of crude currently on the water to create immediate market supply.
  • Energy Sovereignty: The "drill baby drill" policy is framed as the primary driver of U.S. energy security, allowing the U.S. to act as a global "energy superpower" during Middle Eastern instability.

Military Escalation and Tactical Objectives

  • Major Bombing Campaign: Bessent confirmed that the U.S. is launching its largest bombing campaign against Iran "tonight."
      • Primary Targets: The strikes are designed to degrade Iranian missile launchers and the factories responsible for building them.
      • Strategic Goal: To substantially degrade Iran's military capacity and end what Kudlow described as a "47-year war" against the United States.
  • Dismissal of Iranian Naval Capability: Both Kudlow and Bessent characterized the Iranian Navy as "deep underwater" or ineffective, dismissing the threat of small motorboats harassing large tankers.

Geopolitical and Economic Outlook

  • Countering Economic Chaos: Bessent argues that because Iran is losing the military battle, they are attempting to create global economic chaos via shipping disruptions.
  • International Architecture: Kudlow asserted that the current administration's actions are "changing the whole international architecture" by bringing freedom and prosperity to the Middle East through a combination of military force and energy dominance.
