The Bold Plan to Make Tech Serve the Nation—And the Founders Who Are All In | NYNext

New York Post(2.4M) ·57:41 ·718 views · 2026-03-06
Lydia Moynihan (Reporter, New York Post) David Ulevitch (General Partner, Andreessen Horowitz) Katherine Boyle (General Partner, Andreessen Horowitz)
Andreessen Horowitz’s "American Dynamism" practice is capitalizing on a generational and geopolitical shift, moving venture capital toward "hard" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—to bolster U.S.
TL;DR Andreessen Horowitz’s "American Dynamism" practice is capitalizing on a generational and geopolitical shift, moving venture capital toward "hard" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—to bolster U.S. national resilience through profitable, high-tech innovation. ...

TL;DR

Andreessen Horowitz’s "American Dynamism" practice is capitalizing on a generational and geopolitical shift, moving venture capital toward "hard" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—to bolster U.S. national resilience through profitable, high-tech innovation.

The "American Dynamism" Investment Thesis

  • National Interest Focus: Katherine Boyle and David Ulevitch lead a practice area dedicated to companies supporting the national interest, specifically in aerospace, defense, energy, transportation, and manufacturing.
  • Capitalism vs. Charity: Boyle emphasizes that this is not "charity work." For a defense industrial base to be lasting and enduring, companies must be profitable, self-sufficient, and attract top-tier talent via the capitalist system.
  • The SpaceX Blueprint: SpaceX is cited as the foundational success story that proved the private sector could build physical-world technology faster and more efficiently than traditional incumbents.
  • Strategic Fund Growth: The practice recently raised a $1.176 billion fund, marking a transition from a contrarian "lone crew" philosophy to a cornerstone of the firm’s long-term strategy.

A Generational and Cultural Pivot in Silicon Valley

  • The Shift from "Bits to Atoms": Following COVID-19 and the Russian invasion of Ukraine, there has been a sea change in founder interest. Engineers are moving away from optimizing "ad clicks" toward solving physical reality problems.
  • The "Zoomer" Work Ethic: Boyle identifies a distinct cultural shift in Gen Z founders compared to Millennials.
      • Resilience: She describes the younger generation as "not afraid of hard work" and motivated by the "struggle" of building difficult physical products.
      • Inspiration: Younger founders view figures like Elon Musk (SpaceX) and Alex Karp (Palantir) as the primary archetypes of success, rather than the "Harvard dorm room" app-builder model.
  • Apolitical Patriotism: While many founders may not be explicitly political, there is a growing "bottom-up" desire to build things that make America functional and resilient.

Manufacturing and the AI Renaissance

  • Reshoring Industry: Ulevitch argues the U.S. is in a "transformation moment," reversing 40 years of offshoring. This is driven by the need for supply chain stability discovered during COVID-era interruptions.
  • AI as a Labor Multiplier: AI and automation are not viewed as job killers but as solutions to the national talent crisis.
      • Lowering Barriers: In companies like Hadrian, automation allows workers without years of apprenticeship to perform high-level machining.
      • Efficiency: Advanced software allows for more robust, high-margin, and high-output production that can compete globally without solely relying on protective tariffs.
  • Interdependency of Sectors: The partners argue that national security, space dominance, and energy independence are now "linked at the hip"—one cannot succeed without the others.

Operational Strategy and the Future of Work

  • Navigating Washington D.C.: a16z maintains a massive presence in D.C. to assist portfolio companies with regulatory hurdles and government procurement.
  • Modernizing Government Procurement: Ulevitch notes a shift in government leadership, citing tech-sector veterans like Emil Michael (CTO at the Department of Defense) who are pushing to let the best technology win rather than favoring legacy incumbents.
  • Remote Work Debate:
      • Builders: Ulevitch believes founders and builders need to be in-person to brainstorm and iterate on physical products.
      • Investors/Flexibility: Boyle advocates for the broader benefits of remote work, noting its critical role in supporting family formation and increasing birth rates by eliminating commutes for parents.
"The private sector is a very good place for attracting talent, for making sure that you're building as quickly as possible... you want to bring that incentive system to the most patriotic goods, which are aerospace, defense, to the things that really protect the American people." — Katherine Boyle
"We’re entering a great next 250 years for this country... it's a national imperative, but it's also a massive opportunity. You have the founders, you have the demand signal from the government, and you have policymakers finally recognizing the need for doing things differently." — David Ulevitch
"There’s something about this generation that loves their country, that wants to put in the hours, that wants to do the hard work... they want to build hard things. They don’t want to build easy things." — Katherine Boyle
Katherine Boyle, David Ulevitch, you are both partners at Andreessen Horowitz where you manage the American Dynamism practice. Katherine, a lot of this came after you had written back in 2022 that the only way to reverse the course of stagnation and kickstart nationwide renewal post-COVID is through technologists building companies that support the national interest. If I’m correct, that was sort of the kernel that later became what you’re building now. So I would just love to hear a little bit more about what went into that and then how you two connected and were like, "okay, this is it, this is what we’re going to do." Yeah, well I think that, you know, the piece that we wrote in 2022 was in some ways fortuitous because it was three weeks before Russia invaded Ukraine. It was talking about how national security is still important and investing in the national interest is very important. And so it was good timing. But the truth of the matter is, this started much earlier for Andreessen Horowitz and for David and myself. We were investing in this category that we call investing in the national interest—companies like aerospace, defense, national security, energy, and transportation—since 2015 or 2016. These are sort of physical world industries. And, you know, we were sort of the lone crew of people who were saying these things are important. David and I actually met competing to both invest in the Series B of Anduril. We got to split it, and I don’t think either of us were too satisfied with that outcome, which goes into sort of the lore of how we ended up working together. But what I think that piece really did, and why I think it resonated in 2022, is not only because people were sort of waking up to why national security is important. I think there was sort of this view in Silicon Valley that we would have no more wars. When you see a country like Russia invading Ukraine, it woke people up. It woke young engineers up to say these are really important categories that we need to be investing in. I also think people were coming out of COVID and realizing that your physical reality matters. Silicon Valley is not just about building in the land of bits; it needs to be about building in these hard categories that had really been lost for generations. Founders were told there's no reason to build in those categories, or that no one’s going to back you if you want to build a defense company or an aerospace company. And this is a sea change that's been happening now for 20 years. We like to point to SpaceX as sort of the first company to really go out and say, "okay, we can build in the physical world." You look at where SpaceX is now, and time and time again they have proved the naysayers wrong. So I think this is a category that's going to define the next 25 years of innovation. We’re seeing founders just flock to building in the physical world and building in the national interest. Yeah, yeah. David, your thoughts on splitting the round with Anduril? Well, sure. I mean, neither one of us wanted to split the round, but I think we very quickly discovered over the next year or so after that, that hey, we had some very strong alignment in what we believed in. We aligned both in the opportunity and in just the importance of how we spend our time. We thought there would be a huge surge in founders that had the same perspective. If you think back to that era, this is after Google had bailed out on Project Maven. I think for a lot of founders, that was a wake-up call to say, "hey, wait a minute, not only are the roots of Silicon Valley centered in working with the government, but there actually is a huge need to actually support the government and the national interest." We need to make sure that the way of life that we've grown accustomed to is a way of life that continues and actually accelerates. And so, the demand was there, and I think that then the founders recognized that there was an opportunity to actually provide that supply. And I think the other thing that coupled with it is, you know, we were sort of at the very early innings of this technology watershed moment with AI. We saw abilities to transform the way we do manufacturing, the way that computer vision works and can drive autonomy, and the way that we were really changing our views on electrification. We recognized, as Katherine mentioned, that not only is it a defense issue, but it's actually just a national resiliency issue. We had supply chain interruptions during COVID that we needed to respond to. So we needed to be able to re-shore manufacturing and stabilize our supply chains. A lot of that meant bringing things back to the United States for manufacturing, but at the same time introducing advanced software to drive that so that we could do it in a more efficient way, in a stronger way, and in a more resilient way. We want to have much more control over our future destiny. Yeah, and then in terms of working with Katherine, we met during Anduril. We talked about deals and we had philosophical alignment. I certainly realized at some point it would be better to be partners with her than competitors with her. She’s a brilliant mind and a great investor. I convinced her that look, for our firm, even though we had been doing these things sort of piecemeal over the years, we were going to make a full long-term commitment to American Dynamism. We really wanted to make this a cornerstone of the firm. Marc and Ben were very supportive of that. It started out as a practice area in a big umbrella where we made investments out of our venture fund. Then we went ahead and raised our own fund that we’ve been deploying, and we’re now on our second fund. I think that if you look at the overall movement and the fund, you can see that it continues to gain more and more momentum. More founders are building, more policymakers are taking note, and there is more international recognition. So it's an exciting time to be at the early innings of what I think, as Katherine said, will be a 25-year plus journey. It is so interesting though because when you launched this, this was very much a contrarian thesis. And David, as you mentioned, these big tech companies were getting pressure from their employees that, oh, you can’t work with the U.S. government—you could work with the CCP, but the U.S. government was a bridge too far. And so this was very unique, and in a way, I've got to think it was less competitive in the space then than it is now. You have raised another fund, fittingly a billion 176 million. How is the approach to investing now? Obviously, you have a lot more money now, and it’s at a time where we’ve seen Palantir execs like Shyam now volunteering with the military. There’s such a more willingness and patriotism, I would imagine, in Silicon Valley. So has that been challenging in a way that now you have more people competing against you in this space? Yeah, look, I've always believed that time is our most valuable currency and how you spend that time is the most important thing. So for us, spending our time on American Dynamism always made sense. There’s lots of ways to make money, and investing in China was never going to be the way that I was going to make money. It was never going to be the way Katherine was going to make money. We’ve had that perspective long before the American Dynamism practice existed, and in fact, our firm broadly has that perspective as well. I think that because we’ve had that view, it allowed us to sort of plant the seeds of this idea of investing in these areas where software meets hardware and where working with the government comes together. And because we’ve been able to do that, while yes, there’s more people interested in these areas now, there’s more people working for the government, which is terrific by the way. You want the smartest people working for the government. But because we’ve been doing it for so long, we now have a massive presence in D.C. We have a whole team that helps our companies figure out how you sell to the government, how you work with regulators, and how you change policy to make sure that it's a more level playing field. I think that advantage allows the founders to recognize that we’re a great firm to work with, but it also allows us to make the most impact across the board. We introduce the best and most advanced technologies and put them in the hands of our warfighters and our government employees. We make sure that the best technology is not just designed for optimizing ad clicks, but the best technology is designed to make America the best place it can be. Can I add one thing to that? I also think we recognized that competition is good for the consumer and competition is good for the citizen. So if every single firm in Silicon Valley turns away from the CCP and says we want to invest in America, yes, it makes our jobs slightly harder to be in a more competitive sector, but it's very good for the American citizen. So I think it's really important that we point out—we are very aware that Andreessen Horowitz has a massive megaphone. If we say we’re doing something, if we literally plant the flag and say we are investing in America, other investors will follow. And yes, it makes our day jobs a little bit harder, but that's very, very good for America. And it has always been sort of our core hope and view of what success would be: that if we can make sure that every firm in America has an American Dynamism practice or something like it, where they're saying we’re investing in the national interest, this will be a defining category for innovation. It will be a lasting and enduring category for innovation. So I love that you’re noticing that we’re being slightly selfless on our end by wanting to see competition in the sector, but I think it's really good for the American population and really good for our country if we are investing in these categories and we have every investor saying we want to invest in America. It's interesting hearing you talk because there is—I’m not going to say a dual mandate—but there is the sort of balance that I’ve heard you speak about and write about before. It's just sort of balancing patriotism with also obviously investing in great companies. And I know it doesn't seem like there's any conflict there, but how do you sort of balance those two competing goods? And obviously again, as you mentioned SpaceX and Anduril, these are some of the hottest companies. But when you're approaching something, presumably the companies sort of have to get approved on both of those counts. How do you balance those two competing goods though? So I actually love this question because I do think there is a movement. You know, the Department of War had talked about this for many years—like, it would be great if patriotic Americans would invest in these companies even if they’re not making money. And that is not how America works. That is not the capitalist system. We have the best system in human history for creating value, for creating technology, and for making America the country that it is, and that is capitalism. And so yes, you have to make sure as an investor that you are investing in the companies that are self-sufficient, that can grow, that attract the best talent, and that can be enduring. That is the only way that you build a lasting defense industrial base. You can’t just invest in nonprofits and think that they’re going to be able to fight a war. You have to invest in the technology that's going to work. And I think that is what Elon Musk ultimately figured out with SpaceX—that the private sector is a very good place for attracting talent and for making sure that you're building as quickly as possible. The competitive ecosystem that Silicon Valley is, where every day matters, where you're working 20-hour days, where you're sleeping on the factory floor—you want to bring that incentive system to the most patriotic goods. Those are aerospace, defense, and the things that really protect the American people. And so we very much believe that it's our duty to invest in the best companies. We don’t invest in people just because we like them or just because they’re patriots or just because we think that the government needs something. We have to believe that it's going to be the next SpaceX or the next Anduril or the next Palantir. And if we don’t have that belief, it's actually going to do a disservice to the country and a disservice to the Department of War. The reason why this sector is so important now is because we finally have a group of founders—we finally have enough founders who’ve gone to the "University of Elon Musk." They’ve worked with Dr. Karp at Palantir, they understand how to build extraordinary companies, and they are hungry to build companies in the national interest. And we didn’t have that 10 years ago. So I think it's really important for people to understand this is not just a capital play where if enough people throw money at a problem, it's going to build enduring companies. You have to have those magic ingredients of capital, talent, and the right moments to be able to build these companies that are going to last for the next hundred years. Yeah, David. It's interesting too and Palmer Luckey’s spoken about this. He said he maybe doesn't even—you know, in another alternate universe he would love to be investing more in virtual reality. He feels really sort of an obligation to invest and spend his time creating these necessary technologies. I’m curious, do you see founders now sort of feeling a patriotic obligation to create these companies that are going to serve the national interest? Yeah, I think the best founders now look for where they can apply their talents and their ability to attract other talent, and have an impact that they feel is really worthwhile while building a great business. Like Katherine said, this is not charity work for us, but I think that the surface area of great opportunities actually gets larger every day; it doesn’t get smaller. There’s more opportunity, whether it's around extracting minerals and resources that we can bring back to the United States for producing things like batteries and motors, or whether it's around energy generation, power, transportation, and storage of energy. These are things that originally, when we first started the American Dynamism practice, we didn’t know if they would be in thesis. We didn’t know if there would be opportunities, but actually the founders are the ones that come to us with these great opportunities. They say, "hey, wait a minute, I actually think there’s a way to build a huge business." They look at industries where traditionally the incumbents are very, very stodgy and old. They do things with pen and paper and don’t apply advanced software and technology to do it better and in a more robust way, or a higher margin way, or a higher production output way. They don’t drive automation. And so the founders come to us and they see these huge opportunities. They're the ones who actually generally open our eyes to where there’s more and more opportunity and bigger opportunities to benefit not just the country but create these amazing businesses. And so, you know, when someone like Palmer says, "hey, there needs to be a new bank," obviously there are banks. He started Anduril, he started Oculus, and now he’s started a bank. It's like, well, why does he start a bank? Because he sees that there’s an opportunity. There’s a gap in the market where he can actually apply a new approach and an approach that better supports the businesses of America. And so, you know, that’s where they started Harbor, for example. I think that the best companies are started out of necessity where you have great talent that really identifies something wrong with the status quo or a part of the overall landscape that's not being addressed. Yeah, I know we do talk a lot about defense—I’m sure that takes up most of your time—but you also invest in other sectors like energy and public safety. Where do you think there can be the biggest breakthrough? What is a sector that you think hasn’t gotten as much investment that you really want to see more innovation in? Well, I'll give my answer and then Katherine can give hers. Well actually, I would say a lot of these sectors have historically had investment, but not investment into the best founders or at the right time. Sometimes it's the wrong time to start a company, and you need there to be a technology transformation moment to make it work. You know, a lot of people now will use food delivery services and grocery delivery services, but in the first dot-com boom, those all existed. We had Webvan.com, but it didn’t work because we didn’t have the iPhone and we didn’t have ubiquitous connectivity and we didn’t have all of the big data systems to handle all the logistics. But we do have those things now, and now you can sort of pull this remote control for your life out of your pocket and order food and get it to show up to your doorstep. And so you have to start the companies at the right time. So a lot of these categories that we look at, like public safety, energy, and mining—it's not that companies haven’t been started in the past or that people haven’t poured money into these areas before, but they either had the wrong teams or they had the wrong timing. Or in most cases, probably both. So we do think that there's a lot of surface area where we are excited. I think manufacturing is one where there's still tremendous opportunity. It's an area where there has been historically lots of investment, but we’re now in a moment where AI is going to transform the physical world and where robotics is becoming much more real than it ever has been. And so I think everything from manufacturing to logistics is going to be rapidly transformed and create a lot of excellent and incredible companies. Yeah, Katherine, would love to hear your thoughts on that. And also, you know, you’ve written a bit about sort of AI and affordability. Obviously, the solution previously for things to be expensive is technology coming in and making things cheaper. I’ve spoken with some folks at the White House who see this potentially as a solution to inflation and everything getting so expensive, and they see implications for housing and healthcare. What are your thoughts on that and what timing do you think we could see for AI and technology making things more affordable? Absolutely. Well I think in our world—and I’ll go back to David's point on manufacturing... Sorry, we're piling all the questions on you. Yeah, no, no. No, it's great because I actually think it does all fit together. Bringing automation and AI into the factory is transformative in ways that I think a lot of people don’t recognize because it allows another group of people who have not been trained in manufacturing, who have not been trained in machining, who have not done three to five years of apprenticeships, to come into a factory on day one, learn how to build a product, and ship it very quickly. And we actually have a company in our portfolio called Hadrian that was very early on this automation in the factory thesis. When people hear automation in the factory, they think, "oh well you’re not going to need workers." It's actually the opposite. You’re going to need more workers, but you’re not going to have to train them in a way where if they make a tiny mistake when they’re machining a part or putting something together, then you have to restart the process all over again. You’re working with machines that are, you know, 70, 80, 90 percent automated and the machinists are doing the last 10 percent of the work. And that allows an entirely new generation, an entirely new crop of people, to come in and learn how to manufacture products. And I think that is sort of a good example of what AI is going to do across physical industry, which is that we have a talent crisis in the country. As you mentioned in manufacturing, it is extremely expensive, not just because of the talent, but because we do not have the capacity to build things in this country like we used to. And if you could bring automation to do the hard work, you can bring in more talent, you can pay people better wages on building these things, and you can make sure that you produce faster, quicker, and cheaper. The one other thing that I’d like to say about how manufacturing goes together with defense is that you do not have manufacturing in a country that does not have a vibrant defense industrial base. Defense and manufacturing are linked at the hip. I think people don’t often realize this, but you have to be building in the national interest and you have to be building for the Department of War if you want the machine shops and the downstream mom-and-pop shops and all of the things that you need in order to build a part to be vibrant in this country. And so I think in some ways, by investing in the defense side of a lot of these businesses, you’re also getting the benefit on the consumer side for a lot of other products that haven’t been manufactured in the U.S. for a very long time. And we’re going to see the long tail of that effect for 10, 20, 30 years from just investing in the defense sector because so many of these factories are building for things that are not defense as well. And so I think we’re very excited that artificial intelligence and automation is coming to these factories because it's ultimately going to create more jobs and it is going to, as you said, make a lot of products much cheaper for the American consumer. Well, and it's interesting too because I mean, there’s lots of conversation even the last few days with a bit of a tariff snafu from that Supreme Court ruling. But it seems like what you’re outlining extends far beyond basically the tariffs trying to encourage manufacturing. It seems like in a way that you don’t necessarily need the tariffs because this is sort of a long-term investment in the U.S. Any thoughts on that, Katherine? Well, I’ll say, for a lot of our companies, they have to manufacture in the U.S. because they’re regulated by government laws that require them to be built by American citizens in the U.S. And so that is a huge part of what our companies have to do. But I think the downstream effects of investing in more factories and more talent that can build in these spaces is that you’re then going to see the same diaspora that we’ve seen out of SpaceX. Twenty years ago, a small group of people got together and said, "we want to build rockets." And now I would say that every venture-backed manufacturing or defense or aerospace startup, or anything building in the physical world, has a SpaceX talent team that is building for production. And you can say that this next generation of companies that are building in defense—they’re also going to have this massive diaspora over 10 to 20 years that are going to be building factories and building in so many different areas. So I think in some ways if you start the factory and it is built for defense, it's ultimately going to have extraordinary impacts on other parts of manufacturing in this country that we just haven’t seen because, candidly, people just were not investing in these categories. There wasn’t the talent for it, there wasn’t the interest in it. I really think it's generational. We can get more into what I think’s changed about the culture because I do think it is a cultural thing that young people today want to build hard things. They don’t want to build easy things. They want to build hard things, they want to work with their hands, and they want to do cool stuff. And if you have that sort of germ of "we’re going to build for our country," it doesn’t go away. The capital follows it. And so I think that's ultimately what we’re seeing: a movement to do hard things. That’s very exciting. And David, I know you’ve spoken a bit about how you really think this is sort of a magical moment in time where there is this technology renaissance. What are you most hopeful that we will see in the next few years? What are you most excited about that you’re investing in right now that maybe you haven’t gotten a chance to speak about yet? I do think this is a unique moment in time. We’re in a moment where we have this rapid change in the technology landscape where we’re able to put to use AI, computer vision, autonomy, and very large data analytics systems. We can put those things together to apply them to very hard problems. I think that areas that we’ve previously thought were really more discrete—things like energy, manufacturing, space, and defense—actually have a very high overlap in the Venn diagram. You cannot have a strong national defense without a strong defense industrial base and manufacturing industrial base. You cannot have strong national security without space domain dominance, space communications, and total space domain awareness. You cannot have a strong defense without robust, reliable, and transportable energy and power. We have this insatiable thirst for energy that drives a tremendous amount of productivity. And so all these categories just have very deep veins that founders are starting to mine. And if you look back at the last 40 years of offshoring things, we’re now in a transformation moment where we’re reshoring these things. And at the same time, we have a government that is now really recognizing a need to reshore things and to support the defense industrial base. They are supporting our electrification efforts, space domain dominance, and space domain awareness to improve communications and capabilities. And so that does make this a magical moment and a great time for founders and builders to be attracted to these very large categories. And frankly, these are the categories that are the foundation of our entire way of life and our economy. So while we said it's not charity work that we’re doing—we expect these to be massive businesses. Without them succeeding, there's not a lot of stuff on top of it that will succeed either. So it's a national imperative, and I would say even broader, a global imperative for us and our allies. But it's also a massive opportunity. So you have the founders, you have the demand signal from the government, and you have policymakers finally recognizing the need for doing things differently. And in fact, you’ll see if you look at the Department of War, a lot of the leadership comes from the tech sector. You have people like Emil Michael, who is now the CTO of the Department of War, and he’s just done an incredible job of really saying, "hey look, we’re going to procure things differently, we’re going to bring new technologies in differently, we’re going to let the best companies win, and we’re not going to just favor the legacy incumbents just because they’re the incumbents." Yeah, and Katherine, I want to get back to something you were just saying—that you feel this younger generation has sort of experienced a cultural shift. Because I think it's easy to look at old-school Silicon Valley, and while they now seem to sort of be embracing this moment of Trump being in office, there’s a question of: is it really opportunism or is it a genuine change where they want to be more patriotic and see America thrive? But it sounds like maybe that’s not even the right question to be asking of what old-school Silicon Valley focused on because you’re seeing among sort of younger founders a cultural shift. I would love to hear you kind of speak more about that. I love that you asked this question because I do think people outside of Silicon Valley think that it's top-down—like there are certain factors that happen at a macro level and that’s how we make decisions. We are so bottoms-up. We talk to young people and what young people say is the future of what things are going to look like in 10 years, and that’s how we make decisions. And actually, I think the best quote that summarizes why I’m so bullish on the Zoomers is Alysa Liu after winning her gold. She said, “I love to struggle, it makes me feel alive.” And I think that video of her saying that—that she just loves the struggle and that the struggle brings her joy—I think that’s been the word of the Olympics: that she’s so joyful when she skates. When you meet these young people who are like 20, 21, or 22, they all act like that. It's the opposite of the millennials. It's truly—I can say this because I am a millennial—it's the opposite of sort of the morose and the theater kid vibes that we got from the millennial generation where everything was very different in how they operated. And so I think there’s something about the young people of today. Maybe it's that they went through COVID, or maybe it's that they're in many cases the children of Gen X, and they have a totally different vibe of what they care about. But there’s something about this culture of young people coming up where they are not afraid of hard work. They are not afraid to pop in a Zyn and work at the factory all day. They’re not afraid to go to the rink after they've said they've retired. You know, like Jack Hughes—they get their teeth knocked out and they come back and say it wasn't even a question. "Of course I got my teeth knocked out, it’s hockey, right?" Like, there's something about this generation that loves their country, that wants to put in the hours, and that wants to do the hard work. And that means that we’re seeing totally different companies than we saw out of the Facebook diaspora, which was very much the Harvard dorm room: "I like to work on my computer, I like to build apps." It's a totally different style of founder. And for me, it's super exciting to see these young people saying this is how they want to spend the next 10, 20, or 30 years of their life. It's a totally different Silicon Valley than we saw 10 years ago. Wow. And so, I mean, this question that I think a lot of people on the right have had about—you know, if we have a Democrat elected, are we going to see again more YouTube censorship or more of these platforms censoring? But do you think maybe that doesn't even matter because the next generation is so patriotic and sort of bullish on the American project? Yeah, I mean, I think this generation cares a lot about the country and it shocked us. I mean, David and I talk about this all the time. For some of these young people, they were not born on September 11th. They have no recollection of the things that the millennials remember, or anyone older than us remembers. But they care about country, they care about building the hard thing and doing the hard work, and in many cases, I don't think politics is even on their radar. Like, they just think it's cool to build factories. They grew up as children of the internet and they found things that inspired them on YouTube or on whatever platform they were on. They learned the old-fashioned way. It's a very different style of founder than the founder who fought really hard to get into a top-tier university so that they could learn how to code and then drop out and go to Silicon Valley. There’s a different group of people that are building in American Dynamism, and their joy is infectious and their hard work is infectious. They look up to people like Elon Musk and Alex Karp—people who have been doing the hard thing for 20 or 30 years—and they want to do it too. And so I think it's just a different generation of founder that we’ve had the privilege of seeing very early on. I think the rest of the country is going to define tech and Silicon Valley by these people for the next 10 or 20 years. Yeah, David. Your thoughts on splitting the round with Anduril? Yeah, I think one thing that I think is important is that Katherine touched on this: we are very honest and direct. I also think that we have a very strong moral compass which allows us to do these things, to take big risks with conviction, and to really push the envelope and be sort of the tip of the spear of where the venture capital industry goes. We know that when we say something, people listen, and we take that responsibility seriously. Katherine and I actually love to debate, we love to discuss, and we love to be direct with founders. We’re direct with each other and we’re direct with our team. I think people really respect that and appreciate that, and that is an apolitical way of living. That’s not a political way of living. It would be disingenuous to say we’re not political. We are. But we don't agree on everything politically. We agree on making America the place where we both want to live, where we want to have our families, and where we want people to build the best companies. And I think that dynamic really works really well and actually benefits our founders incredibly well. It benefits America incredibly well. It means that we get to be very disciplined when we do make big bets. We do it with very strong conviction and we support the companies through thick and thin. If you talk to founders that work with us, I think they’ll tell you that we always give it to them straight. We try to be as direct and straight as possible. When we think they’re doing well, we support them and we help them and we try to make it go faster and do better. We support them to attract even better talent and raise more money and produce more of whatever they’re building. And when it’s not going well, we tell them too. I think the worst thing that investors can do is not give the truth to a founder and let them know like, "hey, this is not working." You either need to change, do something differently, or hire different people—whatever it is. But if you don't tell them, you don't give them a shot to course correct and to fix it. So I think our dynamic has worked really well. I think the team that we’ve built is incredibly strong. It’s a team like Katherine mentioned—they come from all kinds of backgrounds. Some people have very technical backgrounds, some people come from the government, some people come from the military, and some people have secret clearances. Some people are professional networkers. Having that kind of heterogeneous group makes us very, very strong and able to see the entire landscape that we care about. Yeah. I’m also very curious to get your take—now in New York it feels like everyone's obsessed with back to the office. You are both remote, to an extent. Maybe this is a place where we can actually have a debate, because I think David and I disagree on this. Yeah, I used to have my Twitter location—you know how you say where you live—I used to say "2A," which was like the second row on the plane by the window. Oh, good one. Okay. Because I travel around. I think when you're an investor it's different than being a builder. Look, starting a company is very hard. My belief is that when you're starting a company and you're figuring things out and you're building something and producing something, you kind of all need to be in the same room working together. That’s really important. I think when you're an investor, you need to go find the founders, which means you're often traveling or getting on a plane. You're doing lots of Zoom meetings and you're constantly meeting with people. And for our business, Katherine and I both spend a tremendous amount of time in D.C. So we're often on planes or trains to D.C. in meetings. We're often on Zooms with policymakers, with people inside the Department of War, and people in the intelligence community. And so that job can be done from many places; that doesn’t always require us to be in the same room. At least for our business, it doesn’t require us to be in the same place. But I think it is different, and this is not a double standard, it's just that they're different kinds of jobs. I think it's different when you're starting a company. You want to be in the same room sharing a pizza, talking about things, brainstorming, debugging, viewing the production output, and then going and iterating as quickly as possible. Katherine, you want to talk about it? Yeah. But I also want to talk about work from home because I’ve written a lot about this. I think it is the most important thing that a company can offer to its employees for family formation. A new study came out a couple weeks ago showing that the only thing that has really moved the needle in terms of benefits and government policies is work from home. It eliminates the commute and often allows mothers to work from home when they’re mothers of young children. So I think this is something that parents—and again, as David said, if you’re starting a rocket company, you can’t work from home. That’s not feasible. That is not how you’re going to build rockets. But if you're working at a company where a lot of the work can be done at home, it actually does have downstream effects on how families are formed in this country. Looking at the birth rate right now, I think it's 1.6 in the U.S. Looking at it across the industrial world, the one thing that truly can move the needle is working from home. And so, it's not necessarily true of startups where you have to work 20-hour days, but it is true of a lot of companies. And I think it's something that, as a benefit to incentivize people to come work for you, a lot of CEOs should look to offer. I think it's something that with AI in particular, you’re going to see a lot more people starting businesses from their homes. Inside the Iran War and the Pentagon's Feud with Anthropic with Under Secretary of War Emil Michael

All-In Podcast(1.0M) ·82:59 ·255.6K views · 2026-03-06

TL;DR Under Secretary of War Emil Michael details the "weeks, not months" strategy for Operation Epic Fury in Iran, framing recent actions as a geopolitical move to gain leverage against China while explaining the Pentagon’s unprecedented decision to designate Anthropic as a...

TL;DR

Under Secretary of War Emil Michael details the "weeks, not months" strategy for Operation Epic Fury in Iran, framing recent actions as a geopolitical move to gain leverage against China while explaining the Pentagon’s unprecedented decision to designate Anthropic as a supply-chain risk due to its refusal to support all lawful military use cases.

Military Strategy and Geopolitical Leverage

  • Operation Epic Fury: The conflict is aimed at disarming the Iranian regime, stopping the supply to terror proxies (Hezbollah, Hamas), and halting nuclear/ICBM development.
      • Duration and Scope: Described as a "weeks, not months" operation with no plans for protracted "boots on the ground" similar to Iraq or Afghanistan.
      • The China Connection: David Friedberg and Chamath Palihapitiya argue that neutralizing Iran and Venezuela provides "maximal leverage" against China, which is heavily dependent on imported oil from these regimes (roughly 20% of their economy). This is seen as a strategic precursor to major diplomatic meetings with China in April.
  • Rules of Engagement (RoE): Michael highlights a major shift in RoE, moving away from "insane" parity-based rules (e.g., matching a combatant’s weapon size) to a doctrine of overwhelming force.
      • Field Judgment: Commanding officers now have greater discretion to use their judgment to win within "red lines" rather than waiting for legal clearance for every battlefield action.

Technological Transformation and Drone Warfare

  • Drone Dominance: Michael notes that drone use in recent operations has reached historic levels. The future of conflict is defined by "drone on drone" and "robot on robot" warfare.
      • Lucas Attack Drones: These low-cost (approx. $50k–$80k), one-way attack drones can travel hundreds of miles, providing mass-attritable firepower.
      • AI at the Edge: While fully autonomous "Skynet" scenarios are not yet reality, AI is being deployed for "Golden Dome" missile defense, where human reaction time is insufficient for intercepting hypersonic threats.
  • The "Discombobulator": While Michael refused to give specifics, he acknowledged the existence of advanced, non-lethal electronic or cyber effects used in the Maduro raid to ensure high-stakes missions succeed with zero US casualties.

The Anthropic Feud and Supply-Chain Risk

  • Supply-Chain Risk Designation: In an unprecedented move, the Pentagon labeled Anthropic a supply-chain risk after the company refused to agree to an "all lawful use" clause in their contract.
      • Operational Reliability: Michael’s primary concern was the "insider threat" of a software developer or executive shutting down the model or imposing a "moral refusal" during a kinetic conflict.
      • The Maduro Incident: Tensions peaked when an Anthropic executive allegedly questioned whether their software was used in the Venezuela raid, implying it might violate their terms of service.
  • Vendor Comparison:
      • Grok (xAI): Fully compliant with "all lawful use" across all networks.
      • Google & OpenAI: Working toward compliance; OpenAI CEO Sam Altman reportedly tried to mediate the dispute to prevent a "supply-chain risk" designation for the industry.
  • The "Constitution" Clash: Michael argues that Anthropic’s "Constitutional AI" allows the company to impose its own soul/morality over the US Constitution, which is unacceptable for the Department of War.

Economic Resilience and Industrial Base

  • Maritime Insurance: Following the shutdown of the Strait of Hormuz, the US government (via the International Development Finance Corporation) stepped in to provide political risk insurance for maritime trade after private insurers fled. This may permanently onshore the maritime insurance industry.
  • Defense Supply Chain: The US is in the "early days" of reducing dependency on China for critical minerals and batteries. Michael oversees a $200 billion lending authority to domesticate the manufacturing of solid rocket motors, fiberglass, and other components.
  • New Primes: The Pentagon is actively trying to break the monopoly of traditional defense "Primes" (Lockheed, Boeing) by shifting from cost-plus contracts to fixed-price, commercial-style contracts that favor innovative startups like Anduril and Palantir.

Notable Quotes

"The objective is not to have a fair fight... if you're on our side, you want it to be unfair. We’ve gone back to a Colin Powell approach: go all in, have a clear objective, and use overwhelming force." — Emil Michael
"90 percent of the oil that comes out of Iran goes to China... Creating leverage by having significant influence or damage to those supply chains gives the United States footing to negotiate a better deal for America." — David Friedberg
"If you don't want your stuff to be used for Department of War stuff, you shouldn't be selling to the Department of War. It's in the name." — Emil Michael
"This is deplatforming all over again... If you're not figuring out how to be multi-model and agnostic across these models, you're taking on enormous business risk." — Chamath Palihapitiya

Trump said the US will provide political risk insurance for all maritime trade through the Gulf, especially energy. Friedberg, your thoughts on the economic second-order effects that we're starting to experience here and over the next four weeks could be intense and acute. The modern insurance market emerged specifically to solve the risks of maritime trade. So in the 17th century, Lloyd's of London, which was a coffee shop in London where all the maritime traders would get together and they talked about, "hey, what's the safest route so pirates don't get our ship and so you don't run into weather." That's where they would kind of have these conversations and eventually they started underwriting the risks of the shipping routes and giving each other guarantees. They said, "Hey, if you make this route, great, you pay me a certain amount. And if you don't make the route, I'll pay you the lost value." And that's how Lloyd's of London, which is the kind of world's biggest reinsurance market, started. Today, Lloyd's of London has 78 what are called syndicate members. These are kind of these pools of reinsurance that underwrite big crazy risks like maritime insurance for folks that are moving oil tankers through the Strait of Hormuz, which the IRGC just announced they're shutting down. When the IRGC announced that they were shutting down the Strait of Hormuz, there's a significant risk of all the mines going in the strait and the ships getting attacked and blown up, so loss of value. The insurance premium spiked initially from a quarter percent, so point two five percent of the value of the ship to 1.25 percent. So it went up by like five X. And so folks had to pay a lot more of the value of their ship in order to continue the route and get guarantees that they'll make it through and then all of the markets started to shut down. So once the conflict got heavier everyone said "let's shut this thing down." And that's a obviously a massive risk to energy prices globally, which drives inflation and puts US economic security at risk. And so this is a brilliant move. I would say the US government stepped in with the US International Development Finance Corporation, which was actually funny enough started a couple of years ago in 2019 or something like that as a kind of output of one of the agencies that provided credit from USAID. Much talked about USAID. And so they're leveraging the credit capacity of this old USAID agency to go out and say to all the shipping companies, "Hey, we'll give you insurance on your route." And the reason they need it is the shipping companies are levered. They take on debt to buy the ships and the debtors require that they have insurance or else they're not allowed to take the routes because the debtors are ultimately going to be out the money. And so the shipping companies themselves need to have insurance and so this provides a market that has now gone away. Very smart and ultimately a lot of people are saying this could actually reshore or onshore maritime insurance back to the United States and create an entirely new insurance industry here in the US that has historically been served almost exclusively by European syndicates and European partners. And it actually creates a big economic opportunity as this war dies down for American insurance companies and American brokers to basically be the underwriters and the guarantors of this sort of insurance and create a new industry.
