Is This the Biggest Software Shift of the Decade? | a16z Interview - Atlassian CEO

a16z(252.0K) ·54:19 ·10.4K views · 2026-03-06
Mike Cannon-Brookes (Cofounder and CEO, Atlassian) Alex Rampell (General Partner, a16z) Erik Torenberg (Host)
The software industry is transitioning from a 60-year era of "digital filing cabinets" (databases) to an era where software actively executes work; success in this shift depends on moving from static systems of record to dynamic "systems of process" ...
TL;DR The software industry is transitioning from a 60-year era of "digital filing cabinets" (databases) to an era where software actively executes work; success in this shift depends on moving from static systems of record to dynamic "systems of process" while solving the...

TL;DR

The software industry is transitioning from a 60-year era of "digital filing cabinets" (databases) to an era where software actively executes work; success in this shift depends on moving from static systems of record to dynamic "systems of process" while solving the massive design challenge of human-AI trust.

The "SaaS-pocalypse" and Valuation Frameworks

The public markets are currently struggling to value software businesses during a period of high disruption, leading to a "wait and see" investor sentiment. Alex Rampell (a16z) categorizes the risk into three buckets of SaaS companies:

  • Bucket 1: Seats Tied to Outcomes (High Risk): Platforms where pricing is based on human seats performing a task (e.g., Zendesk). If AI can perform the task, the need for seats—and thus the revenue model—collapses unless they pivot to outcome-based pricing.
  • Bucket 2: Seats as a Pricing Proxy (Low Risk): Platforms where seats are used for "fairness" but don't produce the direct outcome (e.g., Workday). These are safer because the software remains the essential system of record, and AI simply makes the record-keeping more efficient.
  • Bucket 3: The Creative Middle Ground: Platforms like Adobe, where AI might reduce some seats but increase the productivity and value of others.

From Filing Cabinets to Systems of Process

Mike Cannon-Brookes (Atlassian) argues that the definition of software is fundamentally changing from a storage medium to a processing agent.

  • The Historical Context: From 1960 to 2022, software was essentially taking a physical filing cabinet and turning it into a database (e.g., Sabre for flight records, Salesforce for CRMs). The cabinet "couldn't think."
  • The AI Shift: The "cool thing" now is that the filing cabinet can do the work itself. Instead of a human retrieving a file from QuickBooks, QuickBooks can actively collect accounts receivable.
  • Input vs. Output Constraints:
      • Input-Constrained: Processes like customer service or legal where the goal is efficiency (you don't want more legal questions, just faster answers).
      • Output-Constrained: Processes like software development or marketing where AI can unlock unlimited creative value and output.
  • The "Vibe Coding" Reality: While Alex Rampell views building entire enterprise systems via "vibe coding" (natural language prompting) as preposterous due to hidden edge cases (e.g., complex state laws), Mike Cannon-Brookes sees it as a revolutionary tool for extensibility—allowing non-technical users to build custom "mini-apps" on top of stable platforms.

The Paradox of Software Pricing

A significant portion of the briefing examines why moving away from "per-seat" pricing is difficult despite the rise of AI agents.

  • Predictability and Fairness: Per-seat pricing persists because it feels "fair" and is highly predictable for budget planning.
  • The "Casino Chip" Problem: Consumption-based "AI credits" are unpopular with enterprises because they feel like opaque casino chips. Customers lose control over costs when a vendor adds a new AI feature that suddenly spikes credit usage without warning.
  • The Outcome-Based Trap: Outcome-based pricing (e.g., "we save you $10 per ticket") is a great sales pitch in Year 1, but creates friction in Year 2 when the customer no longer has a "pre-AI" baseline to compare against.

The Next Frontier: Design, Trust, and Agents

Mike Cannon-Brookes emphasizes that the biggest bottleneck for AI in the enterprise isn't model capability, but design and user trust.

  • The Skeuomorphic Phase: Much like early mobile apps just mimicked desktop websites, current AI is stuck in the "chat box" phase. True AI design requires moving beyond the "Harry Potter spell" (complex prompting) toward seamless agentic workflows.
  • The "50 Interns" Problem: Managing 50 AI agents is like managing 50 interns—if they ask 50 questions a minute to verify their work, the manager becomes the bottleneck. Design must solve how agents "show their work" without being annoying.
  • Atlassian’s Strategy:
      • Teamwork Graph: Using organizational data to give AI context so it understands not just *what* is being done, but *who* is doing it and *why*.
      • Rovo: Atlassian’s new tool that moves from a blank page to a collaborative "chat-and-edit" paradigm, where the AI acts as a researcher and editor rather than just a generator.

Notable Quotes

"The whole history of software from 1960 until 2022 was you would take a filing cabinet and you'd turn it into a database... The cool thing about everything that's happening in AI land is that the filing cabinet can do work." — Mike Cannon-Brookes
"A lot of software is just a set of deterministic rules that have been learned from decades of experience... You can't just replicate them. You replicate them through experience. The idea I would vibe-code my own work day and then run it is terrifying." — Alex Rampell
"Give people a chat box that can do unlimited power and they're like, 'Tell me a dad joke!'... The underutilized capabilities are so big. It's almost trite now to say the models are far ahead of the value they're delivering." — Mike Cannon-Brookes
"The problem of having 50 interns is you get a lot of work done; the problem of having 50 interns is they ask you 50 questions a minute and all you're doing is answering questions." — Mike Cannon-Brookes
Give people a chat box that can do unlimited power and they're like, "Tell me a dad joke!" In the technology world, the underutilized capabilities are so big. It's almost trite now to say the models are far ahead of the value they're delivering. The whole history of software from 1960 until 2022 was you would take a filing cabinet and you'd turn it into a database. The cool thing about everything that's happening in AI land is that the filing cabinet can do work. The idea I would vibe-code my own work day and then run it is terrifying. However, there is a great gain we are seeing internally in extensibility of software using things like vibe-coding. Everyone has been talking about the SaaS-pocalypse. Some people call it the SaaS-tastrophe. As I've said, not every SaaS company is going to thrive through the next decade. We're not here to defend all of software, obviously. The whole history of software from 1960 until 2022 was you would take a filing cabinet and you'd turn it into a database. The first example of this is a company called Sabre systems, which was started in 1960 by IBM and American Airlines. It took the reservation system, which literally was stored in vaults of filing cabinets manned or womaned by lots and lots of secretaries in the 1950s and 1940s. The airlines had been around for a long time, and then it put them in an early SQL database. This was an early database back when 10 megabyte hard drives probably cost like a hundred million dollars. Then that's what happened with electronic health records; the first one was called MOPS. It was built by Mass General Hospital. Then there were the first Siebel systems predating Salesforce, or actually the first CRM which was called Act! systems in 1987. Basically every single filing cabinet became a database. There were benefits to that, but it didn't actually make the world that much more efficient. Before, you would have a human go fetch you the HR file for Eric. You'd say, "Oh, go to the HR filing cabinet and get me that file." Now it's in Workday, but now you have to have a CISO to make sure that your Workday doesn't get hacked. You have to have IT people to provision accounts in your SSO to Workday. Did the world get that much more efficient? It did if you have multiple offices because now people can collaborate. You could do complex joins on a database, which is much harder to do on pieces of paper. But that was kind of software from 1960 to 2022, because the filing cabinet couldn't think for itself. Now, this is the cool thing about everything that's happening in AI land. The filing cabinet can do work. QuickBooks can actually accomplish a task by itself versus just relying on a human to retrieve the file from QuickBooks. This is different from the way a human in 1500 would retrieve a file from the "ye olde filing cabinet" in the "ye olde accounting department." So, it's interesting. It's actually a great segue into what everyone is talking about right now, the SaaS-pocalypse. Some people call it the SaaS-tastrophe. Obviously, what is happening in the public markets is significant. A lot of people have different perspectives of how significant it is or what it means. I want to hear from both of you how you interpret what's been going on and, more importantly, what it means or how we should make sense of it. Why is there too much fear about this, or how should we make sense of this? Look, I think the world is trying to work out how to rate or value software businesses in a highly disruptive stage. Everyone has hot takes about what the future's going to look like. Depending on those takes, you get a version of the future that's either really good or really bad for all of software, or for certain companies and categories in software. It's a really interesting thing. There's no doubt in my mind that the risk level has gone up. If you think about it from an investor mindset, you're like, "This used to be a very stable category, now it's a more risky category, hence I'm gonna step away and watch." As I always say, investors are trying to work out not necessarily the DCF cash flow model of a company for all profits of history. They're really trying to work out what other investors are going to do. They're actually betting on what other people think that other people think they're going to do. Right now, that sort of logically makes sense. You have an interesting world where everyone has a version of what the future is likely to look like and it seems likely to them. It's pretty disconnected from the reality on the ground, but the answer is always: "What if AI can do that in two years or three years? What does that mean?" I think it comes from a very static viewpoint where people won't adapt and the world won't change. It's like one thing is going to change and everything else is going to remain static. You have this interesting world at the moment where businesses like ours are doing very well. We've had three great quarters in a row and everybody says so, and then you're like, "Wait, but that used to equate to some value." It's our job to prove that that's not the case for our business. We're not here to defend all of software, obviously. For our business, we feel very good about the opportunities we have, the data we keep showing, and the results we keep showing. That doesn't mean we don't have to adapt. I always say this as well. It's this weird world where we are changing how we work radically and quickly as we always have. We've been doing that for a number of years. Some part of the current market perspective assumes that we won't be able to change. There are strategic vectors for sure, but the reality is that not every SaaS company is going to thrive through the next decade. Just like a bunch didn't make it to the cloud, and a bunch didn't make it from the Windows era to the internet era, or whichever era you want to say. No one is going to say that 100 out of 100 SaaS companies are going to make it through and be thriving and growing on the other side. Also, we have this version that software kind of dies. A lot of it just ends up as a cash revenue stream. I can speak for us: this is the best thing that's happened to our business. We're in a knowledge world. We have tools to play with that knowledge, to act on that knowledge, and to do all sorts of other things to solve the jobs our customers have always hired us for. Logically, this is very good, but it's up to us to execute that through this transition. I think we're doing that really well, but again, we have to prove that to people over time. The patience part is hard for markets. Alex, how about you? How do you react to what's been happening, or how do you make sense of what's going on? Well, I hope I'm right in the long run, which is that all this stuff is crazy. I think I tweeted about this a few weeks ago. My kind of cursory glance is that there are three different types of SaaS companies, and the public markets couldn't tell the difference between the three. One is where seats are tied to outcomes. These seats are being used by people who use software in a way that goes back to the filing cabinet metaphor. If I'm using Zendesk, for example, they came up with a very clever pricing model. Maybe I can take a step back before I even answer your question. There's this great book by Dan Ariely called Predictably Irrational. I used to give it to all my product managers in my company. I told them to study this to figure out how we charge people for stuff, because it turns out that people operate a certain way. In the example he gives, imagine you're locked out of your apartment at midnight. You hire a locksmith who comes one minute later and lets you in in 30 seconds. He says it's 500 dollars. You're like, "500 bucks? You just did 90 seconds of work!" You leave him a one-star Yelp review, leave no tip, and protest the charge on your credit card. Now imagine a parallel universe. The locksmith comes and spends nine hours trying to let you in. He goes back to his office to get more tools. Finally, in the morning, he lets you into your apartment. You're so grateful that he spent nine and a half hours helping you that you give him a 200 dollar tip and leave him a five-star rating on Yelp. This is an example from the book. It basically means humans are kind of willing to pay for incompetence. A lot of pricing is about fairness. It feels fair that I give that guy more money even though he's completely incompetent, whereas I'm pissed that the competent guy overcharged me. It doesn't make any sense, but it feels fair. If you think about how we got to SaaS, it's per seat per month. In many cases, the additional cost of provisioning a seat digitally is close to zero. It just feels fair. You have 500 seats, so you pay more money than if you have one seat, even though it's kind of the same thing going on in the background. The three types of SaaS companies that I think of—great oversimplification here—start with category one. You have seats, and the seats are being used to produce some element of work. But now, you don't need the seats anymore to produce that work. Zendesk would be patient one there. How many seats does a Zendesk customer need today if they're using Sierra or Decagon, or rolling their own? It's potentially zero. Zendesk is in peril because of the per-seat pricing. If Zendesk said they were just going to charge per seat per month for the current thing and never make a change to their code or pricing, that revenue stream is 100% going to zero. On the other hand, it could triple or quadruple because they might just move to outcome-based pricing and ditch the old model. It still has to be subject to the laws of fairness and being predictably irrational, but for something like Zendesk, it could go up or down. The default path, unless it changes, is going to zero. On the complete other side of that, you might have per-seat pricing because it feels fair, but the seats are not tied to an outcome. Workday has this great pricing model where they say, "Oh, you're GE and you have 340,000 employees? I'm going to charge you per employee per month." Why? I don't know, it just feels fair. But those employees that work at GE are not using Workday to produce an outcome. Workday is fine. In fact, if anything, this goes into what you can do with AI tools. When you hire somebody at GE, they need to do a reference check to make sure you worked at the companies you claimed. An HR person has to go look at the file that's in Workday and go call those three companies. Workday can call those three companies! An AI tool can do that, but only through the system of record. Something like Workday or Intuit—which was down 45% in late February—is solid. Nobody's going to get rid of QuickBooks. These are the two tentpoles. One is where seats are charged and tied to work, and the other is where seats are a pricing trick not tied to work. Then there are things in the middle, like Adobe. Maybe you need more seats, maybe you need fewer, but it's not as stark as the Zendesk example nor the Workday example. Against that, you have this undercurrent of "I'm going to vibe-code everything." I think that is just preposterous, having been a software developer for a very long time. The person I like to cite as my counter-example here is my second favorite economist, David Ricardo. In 1817, he came up with the theory of comparative advantage. You could also grow your own food or weld your own aluminum, but those are bad examples because those things are simple. I have a comparative advantage filming podcasts with you. I could do other things, but I can earn more doing this even if I might be more productive than the plumber. I should still do the podcast. That's actually less important than what I like to call all the edge cases that lie beneath. I could theoretically vibe-code me some Workday, but what happens in Indiana if the person leaves and they're on maternity leave? You don't know about all these edge cases unless you've encountered them in the wild. A lot of software is just a set of deterministic rules that have been learned from decades of experience. The rules are not exposed; they're kind of embedded and you can't just replicate them. You replicate them through experience. I think there are three types of SaaS that might oversimplify the view of the world. Then there is this idea that the IP is worthless because everybody's going to vibe-code their own thing. I think for certain subcategories, maybe that's true if it's a very simple task with no edge cases. However, I think software is going to do great because the true systems of record have sticky software that people rely on. They have all of these embedded edge cases. They're going to start adding AI where AI does the work. Workday will say, "Do you want us to do a background check?" Intuit will say, "Do you want us to go collect on your outstanding accounts receivable?" You don't have to hire humans to do that; you hire your software. That is starting to happen. When it does, the present value of future cash flows is going to go up a lot. It's astonishing to me that a lot of public market investors can't tell the difference between these different buckets. They're not giving any credit for how you deploy AI. You have to deploy AI through software that's a system of record. I think it's a fascinating time for everyone getting to first principles of what a business really does. Personally, I hate the "system of record" thing because it sounds like a database sitting there, very static, where I put stuff in and pull it out. That views a business as a set of filing cabinets in an industrial era kind of world. That was very different than the pre-industrial era, and I get why we have the term. But it feels a little bit like why we have a floppy disk icon as the save button. My kids ask, "What's that?" and I say, "That's a disk," and they're like, "What is that?" I realize they've never actually physically seen a disk, but they still know what the save button does. The reason I question this is that to me, businesses are a set of processes. They're not a system of record. These are all process-based systems. Everything Alex has just said is totally true, but there are processes like reference checking or other things. Your ability to coordinate a set of processes to happen as cheaply and efficiently and quickly as possible is actually your entire business in a knowledge business. I have 10,000-plus people who walk into buildings every day, bring their brains, and walk out. I don't have atoms or bits or steel, and I don't think I even have filing cabinets. I am all about coordinating sets of processes, which I think most modern businesses probably are. When you get to how that relates to Alex's commentary, I think it's totally true. We have different types of processes within a business. There are what I like to call input-constrained and output-constrained processes. The customer service example with Zendesk is input-constrained. Your customers ask a certain amount of questions. How quickly you process those is about your efficiency, cost, speed, and quality. If you do it 10 times as fast, you don't get 10 times as many questions. For every customer, they might ask five questions. You try to make them ask fewer questions or process them quicker. There's a lot in a business that is an input-constrained process. I always use our legal team as an example. Their job is not to generate legal work; it is to answer it. How many leases do we have? How many NDAs? How many contracts? It's a fixed set, and I'm trying to do it as efficiently as possible. But then I have output-constrained work, like anything creative, marketing, or software development and technology. There, I can theoretically do an unlimited amount of tasks. I'm constrained by my creativity and how many things I can think of to do to deliver value for my customers. In those areas, I'll take the efficiency gain and probably do more output rather than limit input. The challenge is to look at a business and try to make this analysis from the outside. All of your input-constrained processes and output-constrained processes work together to make a business. They all have to liaise in interesting ways, and that's where you see pieces of software coordinating humans who are running processes. What you're saying about Indiana is totally true because some of those processes have outside rules, like laws, governance, and compliance. In Indiana, I have to do a certain thing for employees. The processes are both how I want my business to run and how it has to run. The business is really just a collection of all these processes put together. It's a different view from saying, "We have a system of record and a system of action." That's not how I think most businesses actually run. Despite the fact that I love Intuit, let's look at TurboTax. The tax code is published. You can download all of these rules. It's highly deterministic. Then your files are in your messy downloads folder, and the goal is to make those two happen. In that case, everything is actually transparent in terms of the processes. I think it's actually quite a rare situation where the edge cases are published in one or fifty places. There are 50 states, each with its own tax code, plus the federal tax system. You download that stuff and make it work. There probably are still edge cases and processes that you learn, because the real world normally isn't as neat as that. A business has value. There are a lot of businesses where the assets leave every night because they go down the elevator and they go home. Those are knowledge economy type things. But actually, these businesses do have value. Does McKinsey have value outside of all of the employees that work there? That's a knowledge economy business where they produce outcomes tied to labor. But they probably have some top-secret handbook they use for how they hire people, fire people, and produce outcomes for clients. I haven't seen it, and that's actually great because I can't replicate it. It's probably been built over a hundred years. What is it that non-digital products do? Their product is the accumulated knowledge from potentially centuries or decades. I love going to Japan and seeing a noodle store that has been around since 1587. There's probably something going on there. It's an accumulated set of culture and knowledge and know-how besides just a recipe list. Maybe making noodles is an easier example, but maybe there are edge cases. What happens if you run out of flour? How did the noodle shop survive the Great Flour Shortage of 1623? They probably did something and that's accumulated in a secret book of know-how. That is opposed to just replicating something where all of the rules are published to the public. Or maybe like Intuit again—is Intuit filling out the tax code for you? Does Intuit know the tax code as well as anyone else can? What they're helping you with is taking your life data and asking you the right questions. Intuit is almost more like a McKinsey in that way. Their special ability is knowing how to ask you the right questions to fill out the tax code rather than just the filling out of the tax code itself. All these businesses are having to look at their internal processes. Maybe I have 50 processes I think are my secret sauce. Now I have to really consider which ones are actually unique. I think it's also a question of the Goldilocks zone of whether it is worth doing yourself versus not. If you consider the independent variable of, "Should I Claude-code some software by myself?", well, if it's 99% of my cost and my business is going to fail because I'm being overcharged, it might make sense. If it's like a dollar a year, it probably doesn't make sense. Not all systems of record are the same. I think of the system of record as the atomic unit of something for a business. Calendars are a system of record for time, or ERP is a system of record for inventory. If I have an office in Miami that I don't go to very often, and there's a system of record for conference rooms like Google Calendar, am I willing to change that? Yeah, because I only go there once a year. Who cares? Compare that to something that touches my revenue. Am I really going to grow my own food? If you take that farming metaphor, it's actually a lot cheaper to go to a restaurant if I just want one hamburger versus getting a cow and feeding it and waiting. A lot of food is cheaper if you consume it in a restaurant because of comparative advantage and economies of scale. There are some systems of record that are more susceptible just because they overprice or they're just not as valuable in terms of what they're storing. Carta keeps track of cap tables for a lot of companies. How often do you access your cap table? Not very often. But it's super valuable, and you can't mess that up. I'd probably rather use Carta for that and they don't charge that much money. It's not a daily-use kind of product. The vibe-coding thing is so fascinating to me because as someone in software, people say, "Oh, people are just going to vibe-code all these replacements to tools." The idea I would vibe-code my own workday and then run it is terrifying. I have some really smart engineers, but I have other stuff for them to do. I feel like that has way more downside than upside for me. However, there is a great gain we are seeing in extensibility of software using vibe-coding. Most of these applications are highly configurable and customizable. In our case, that goes all the way through to true extensibility. You can write pieces of software and apps that run on top of our platform. Lots of customers do, but those customers need to put a technology team on doing that job. Their ability to vibe-code extensions and tailored applications to their specific use case is the real gain. I might want an app for the Miami team to do conference room booking, and maybe Miami has some weird HR policy, so that app needs to look at Workday and this and that. It's used by 20 people. I probably wouldn't have been able to afford to put the internal IT team on building that because the bill would have been too big. But now maybe I can build that. That uses Workday's data and rules underneath, but it gives me a custom interface for the person on the front desk in Miami to do something specific. That is super powerful, but it's not a replacement for Workday. Poor Workday is like the butt of a lot of these conceptual examples. This actually makes Workday stickier in the enterprise and more valuable because you can build all these applications on top. That's the power of AI and vibe-coding—to make things more tailored for what I need. But we're going to have to be really careful about these layers of stability and rules versus customization. You could argue that OpenClaude and stuff are examples of building very personal apps. Most of those people aren't software developers; they're building apps that work just for them on top of their Gmail or something else. It still uses Gmail as the rails. They still go to Gmail to read their email, but they build some specific thing for themselves to solve a problem they have. A couple of them maybe turn into companies, but most of them are just solving some stuff they needed. That's great and really powerful. That's why I'm curious about the bucket of pricing fairness where the backend is not the frontend. If you think of Salesforce, they charge for licenses. We might have 600 people at our firm and 600 Salesforce licenses. I've never logged into Salesforce, but I bet we pay for me because I use the output of it sometimes. It stores all of our relationships. I am like part of a table in a relational database. We really just want to pay for a database. In a world where the frontend is not the backend, what happens? For Workday, I think they've come up with a very clever pricing trick. "Trick" undersells it. It's a powerful pricing paradigm that feels fair. The more employees that you have, the more you pay. Why is that fair? Because GE has more profits than a 10-person company. GE is going to pay more for this thing. It's still a drop in the bucket. I don't think anybody's going to vibe-code that, and they're going to add all this AI revenue. Most importantly, their pricing feels fair. For things where the frontend is somewhat divorced from the backend, I don't know what the fair format for pricing is. Obviously, if nobody's going to vibe-code their own thing and there's not going to be any competition, then pricing will stay unchanged. But you can imagine a world where people are building things on top to read from the database. Will there be any pricing pressure on any of these categories? For me, I think if the frontend is not the backend, there's more susceptibility than if they're very tightly intertwined. QuickBooks is used by small businesses where the owner just logs into QuickBooks. The frontend kind of is the backend. Compare that to Salesforce, where you can imagine nobody gets rid of it, but maybe they have fewer seats because they need fewer frontends, even though they still need the backend desperately. It depends on how fairness and optics in pricing work. People need to feel like what they pay for relates to their usage. I would say a 10,000 person company paying for Workday—or a 20,000 person company—probably pays twice as much plus some discount because they have twice as much complexity, and they see that as fair. It seems reasonable that I would pay per employee for my HR system. The question is about those business logics. They are not irrelevant. A business has them because it runs as a collection of processes and they want standardization. They want teams to work the same way so someone can manage them and track output. If I have car factories, I want to track the cars in and out consistently. The business logic where it gets baked in is where the value is. The question is, how much do sales-adjacent teams need those processes? I assume Salesforce Sales Cloud has an MCP server. That MCP server doesn't just go to the database; it involves your processes and rules. If someone in marketing or customer success needs those processes and governance, like "Hey we only do X for customers in Japan," then even their MCP server is going to need an account. Whether the customer thinks that's fair is a different question. I tell you, we get this all the time talking about consumption-based pricing, usage-based pricing, or outcome-based pricing. There are a lot of categories where that makes sense, but I do not believe it will be the majority pricing manner for all SaaS software. When you talk to customers, they often hate it when it is not related to the value they put in. I have usage-based pricing for Splunk. If I send them twice as many logs, I pay more money. I get it, but the logging is up to me. I can log more or I can log less. I can tell teams not to log so much if it's expensive. I can control the amount of data I put in. Same with storage in S3. I put in a gigabyte, I put in two gigabytes, fine. Those are relatively transferable and controllable. A lot of the examples people give of outcome or consumption-based pricing are not in control by the customer. The AI token world or AI credit world is really difficult for customers because they don't understand what this "casino chip" they've been given is. I can take a gigabyte from AWS and go put it in Azure and I know how much they're going to charge me because the gigabyte is constant. With AI credits, I don't know if your credits are the same as yours. By the way, you keep adding features which chew up my credits because my users use them, and I'm like, "Wait, I don't know what they're doing with those credits." It's not the company choosing to use them; it's the vendor adding features. I can 10x my customer's credit usage overnight by adding summaries and they might say, "Wait, I didn't do that." I think customers want seats. Today, they understand it, and secondly, they've been burned by consumption-based billing where the bill goes up massively and they don't know how to control it. Right, it's predictable. It will take some adjustment. It will be present in some categories. We have areas of our business at Atlassian that are consumption-based, but we stick to areas where if customers do twice as much stuff, they get twice as much value and it's in their control. A lot of these other things aren't in their control. One last example of outcome-based pricing is that those outcomes are dynamic. If I say, "You used to spend 20 dollars on customer service and with our tool you'll only spend 10," that's a great sales pitch in year one. In year two, the customer says, "But I only spend 10. Now I want to spend five, otherwise you didn't deliver any value." The vendor goes, "Well if you took me out you'd be spending 20." And the customer says, "Wait, but I don't spend 20, I spend 10." My ability to save you money each year is difficult from an outcome basis because I'm eliminating tasks. From a sales perspective, I started two payment companies and I envied Workday. I would talk to my sales team about how they know from the outside in how much money they make from GE. They can say, "GE has 330,000 employees. Maybe we charge them five dollars per employee per month." It's so much easier to scale a sales team if you know a company will pay you three million dollars. When we were starting Affirm, we signed up 1-800-Flowers and had no idea how much we'd make. It turned out Casper the mattress company made the business work. We got Walmart, but it didn't work that well in the beginning. Workday has predictability in both directions. It's predictable for the spender and for the management team knowing they should sign up GE and not a 10-person company. It's crazy in internet land where Stripe might make more money from a 10-person company than GE. When you have outcome-based pricing or consumption-based pricing, it just becomes exponentially harder to scale a sales and marketing team. One thing I want to go back to is how you guys are adapting in this era. Can you share more about how that's manifested for you and how it's made you change your business? Look, we sell collaboration tools that solve human collaboration problems in service teams, broad business teams, HR, finance, and software teams. Fundamentally, they're all collaboration problems that involve a lot of text, so this is really good for us. The technology world often runs to the idea that we're going to reinvent everything. That generally is true in the medium to long arc of time. Our challenge is that we have a lot of customers that work in today's manner, today's workflows, and in today's set of apps. They want to get to tomorrow, but they also have to move a lot of people. When we're building AI features, we need to understand how that technology can help us. Secondly, what fundamental platform componentry do we need to build for whatever that future will be, because this stuff's accelerating so fast? That's how we got to our AI Gateway, the teamwork graph, and the enterprise compliance and controls. You have to separate that from the features you're building for customers in a given app. Then you have to build features for customers that they actually use. Where do you put those features? A whole bunch of them are in existing workflows to help the customer do that existing workflow faster, better, and more efficiently. Those tend to be very unexciting from a magic point of view in terms of what sells a 30-second animated GIF on X, but they're incredibly exciting for the customer because they can use them today. Their existing way of working just got better. In the AI world, people are often like, "But that's pretty simple." But it actually helps them today in a massive way. I tell people internally that's not enough because you also need to look at new workflows and be able to handle those as well. If you look at Jira, summarizing a ticket is something we can do way better than we ever could. In enterprise, maybe four or five people work a ticket internally to try to resolve a problem. The fourth person that shows up has to look at attached files and conversation to understand what's going on. They would normally have taken 30 minutes to read it all. Literally just summarizing that is so powerful, and they haven't changed their workflow one iota. It's still Alex saying, "Hey Eric can you come help me with this ticket?" Eric shows up and has to bootload his brain. That's an existing workflow where we can use LLMs just to make that customer way better. They rave about all these types of features, even though they're simple and usually not agentic. Then we can say, "Cool, well in that service workflow we need to put agents in at various spots." Most people are taking a workflow and finding the steps that trip them up or cost a lot of time. We have to provide agent frameworks ourselves. We have a pretty great agent framework that uses all the teamwork graph and context. It's pretty simple and very affordable. Or you bring your own agent framework. Most businesses will have three to five large-scale agent platforms running internally and they might say, "Hey, I use Agentforce for this or Gemini for this." Great, bring that agent and we'll pop it in the workflow. But what if the service ticket didn't exist at all? You're reimagining whole categories of software and new workflows, and we have to help our customers make it across that gap. They don't generally have one service team; they have hundreds. They might say, "These 20 are going to work in this new way," but they have to manage them all. We're trying to bring data in the teamwork graph together with a customer-driven lens. We're trying to take them five years into the future, but it's our job to actually get them one year and two years and five years into the future simultaneously. The last thing I'd say is we're investing a lot in design. In an AI conversation, that often gets left out. There is a lot of foundational design to do in how this works. We're seeing the first elements of this, but if I look at the mobile era, the first set of apps were taking desktop things and sticking them in a phone. Then we evolved new patterns of experience. We put in push notifications, and drag-to-refresh is a canonical design pattern that was successful and moved across. We have so many design challenges to solve that help people understand what's there. The average customer doesn't necessarily want to understand how the AI works, but they want the outcomes. It's our job to hide the technical detail and just give them the answer or make a task more efficient. In the technology world, we get so obsessed by model quality. It's almost trite now to say the models are far ahead of the actual value they're delivering. A huge part of that equation is actually design and experience. How do I get this to people? You give people a chat box that can do unlimited power and they're like, "Tell me a dad joke." It's very hard to help them utilize that power. I love the skeuomorphic point. First, you have pieces of paper. The early web was just like a webpage, that's why it's called a webpage, it's like 8 and a half by 11. Then mobile came and we made it a tiny webpage. It turns out if you don't just go into the skeuomorphic world but think from first principles, you do other things like pull-to-refresh. That was a new concept. I was thinking about this the other day—have you tried Nano-Banana 2? Yes. It's really good, right? One of my colleagues just said, "Hey, for an American tourist visiting Japan, make an infographic about what to do and not to do," and it one-shotted something that's amazing. How do you edit that output? It feels very skeuomorphic to say you could edit the text or graphics or just one-shot something new. What do you think the state of the art should be for editing AI output? I would zoom out two levels from that to answer that. First, customer trust is really hard in these areas. When you go talk to users and do research, they're often very scared of AI. Not because of its power, but because it does stuff and they're like, "Hey, how do I know that was right and what did it do?" It's like the idea that "Don't worry, my AI bot's gone and sent 15 emails and managed your inbox." You're like, "Okay, did it? I don't trust it yet." I have a trust question on AI doing things really quickly. To gain trust, it has to come back to you and say, "Here's what I'm about to do, are you sure you want me to do this?" without being annoying. That's a whole design question of how you build trust with any of these tools. The second is does it have enough data? So much of AI is one-shotting things. On X, you'll see a thousand posts like, "Hey, this is the magical prompt that runs you a billion dollar business." That's kind of ridiculous because the reality is you also have a lot of iteration on the data side. Right? One-shotting things is really useful, but you often then need to go back and edit the output and the input. Right?
